Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
7 min
Faith Requires Evidence: The Linguistic and Logical Foundation
Nasafi defines faith as affirmation based on evidence, refuting those who accept bare affirmation without evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that faith according to Nasafi is not mere affirmation but affirmation grounded in evidence, supported by logical and linguistic arguments.

Core Idea
Faith is affirmation based on evidence, not bare belief.

Nasafi begins by engaging with those who claim that faith requires evidence. He refutes the idea that any affirmation without evidence can be considered knowledge or faith. Through logical reasoning and linguistic analysis, he shows that true faith must be built on demonstrative proof. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding why rational evidence is essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's position
Read the first sentence to understand the claim that faith requires evidence. Notice that the opponent insists on evidence being conjoined with affirmation.
The opponent is not named but likely refers to rationalist theologians.
2
Follow the refutation using types of knowledge
Nasafi argues that bare affirmation is not knowledge because it lacks a cause. Understand the division of knowledge into necessary and inferential, and see why this affirmation fits neither.
This logical step undercuts the opponent's claim that mere belief suffices.
3
Examine the linguistic analysis of 'iman'
Nasafi traces the word 'iman' to the root 'amn' meaning safety. He explains that true faith involves entering safety through reflection on evidence. Compare this with the phrase 'amantuhu fa-amina'.
This analysis supports his conclusion that bare affirmation is not faith.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
After the linguistic argument, Nasafi concludes that bare affirmation without evidence is not faith. Write down the key premise and conclusion in your own words.
This conclusion sets the stage for the next lesson on rational evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما من يقول : إنما اشترطنا اقتران الدليل بالتصديق ليصير الاعتقاد بدونه مبنيا على الدليل إيمانا، فيحتاج إلى التكلم معهم فنقول : بأنهم يقولون : إن التصديق إذا لم يقترن به دليل، يكون مجرد اعتقاد نشأ لا عن دليل، ومثل هذا الاعتقاد لا يكون علما ، إذ العلم المحدث لابد له من سبب ضروريا كان أو استدلاليا، ولا سبب لهذا الاعتقاد، فلم يكن علما، ولأن العلم المحدث نوعان ضروري واستدلالي لا ثالث لهما، وهذا الاعتقاد لم يثبت عن ضرورة فلم يكن ضروريا، ولا عن دليل فلم يكن استدلاليا ، فلم يكن علما، ومن المحال أن يكون من لا علم له بحدوث العالم، وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته وثبوت الرسالة مؤمنا، وهذا لأن التصديق وإن وجد إلا أن مطلق التصديق ليس بإيمان بل الإيمان هو التصديق المقيد بكونه مبنيًا على الدليل، وهذا لأن الإيمان في حقيقة اللغة ليس هو التصديق بل هو إدخال النفس في الأمان يقال: أمنته فأمن كما يقال : أجلسته فجلس ، فكل من أخبر بخبر فتأمل في دليله فعرف بالدليل صدقه فصدقه يقال : آمنه أي صدقه بعدما أدخل نفسه بالتأمل في دلائل صدقه في الأمان، من غير أن يكون مكذوبا أو مخدوعا أو ملبسا عليه في هذا الخبر، فإذا كان الأمر على هذا تبين أن التصديق العاري عن الدليل ليس بإيمان، وإذا ثبت هذا فبعد ذلك يقول أبو الحسن الأشعري والمعتزلة : إن الدليل لابد من أن يكون عقليا، إذ لا وجه إلى جعل قول الرسول، دليل حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع لأن قول الرسول لا يكون حجة ما لم يثبت رسالته، ولا وجه إلى القول برسالته إلا بعد معرفة مرسله ولن يتهيأ معرفة مرسله إلا بعد ثبوت المعرفة بحدوث العالم وإذا كان كذلك لن يتصور حصول المعرفة بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع بقول الرسول لأن المعرفة بصحة قوله مترتبة على معرفة حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع يحققه أن العاقل ما لم يعرف أن صانع العالم حكيم لا يظهر المعجزة على يد المتنبي، كما يذهب إليه القائلون بأن السفه ما خلا عن العاقبة الحميدة. أو يعرف أن صانع العالم تام القدرة يستحيل عليه العجز ، وظهور المعجزة على يد المتنبي، يعجزه عن
As for one who says: 'We only stipulated the conjunction of evidence with affirmation so that belief without it, if based on evidence, becomes faith,' then it is necessary to discuss with them. So we say: They say that if affirmation is not conjoined with evidence, it is mere belief arising not from evidence, and such belief is not knowledge, because originated knowledge must have a cause, either necessary or inferential, and this belief has no cause, so it is not knowledge. And because originated knowledge is of two types: necessary and inferential, no third, and this belief is not established by necessity, so it is not necessary, nor from evidence, so it is not inferential, so it is not knowledge. And it is impossible that one who has no knowledge of the origination of the world, the existence of the Maker and His oneness, and the establishment of prophethood be a believer. This is because even if affirmation exists, absolute affirmation is not faith; rather, faith is affirmation restricted by being based on evidence. This is because faith in the linguistic reality is not affirmation, but rather it is entering the soul into safety. It is said: 'amantuhu fa-amina' just as it is said: 'ajlastuhu fa-jalasa.' So whoever is informed of news, reflects on its evidence, knows by evidence its truth, and then affirms it, it is said: 'ammanahu' meaning he affirmed him after he brought himself, through reflection on the evidences of his truthfulness, into safety, without being lied to, deceived, or confused regarding this news. So if the matter is thus, it becomes clear that bare affirmation without evidence is not faith. And if this is established, then after that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila say: The evidence must necessarily be rational, because there is no way to make the Prophet's statement the evidence for the origination of the world and the existence of the Maker, for the Prophet's statement is not a proof unless his prophethood is established, and there is no way to assert his prophethood except after knowing his sender, and it will not be possible to know his sender except after establishing knowledge of the origination of the world. And if so, it is not conceivable to obtain knowledge of the origination of the world and the existence of the Maker through the Prophet's statement, because knowledge of the truth of his statement depends on knowledge of the origination of the world. And the existence of the Maker is verified by the fact that the rational person, unless he knows that the Maker of the world is wise, miracles will not appear at the hand of a false prophet, as the proponents of the view that foolishness is what lacks a praiseworthy consequence hold, or he knows that the Maker of the world is perfectly powerful and incapability is impossible upon Him, and the appearance of miracles at the hand of a false prophet would incapacitate Him...
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the position of those who require evidence for faith itself. The author begins by stating that such opponents claim that faith is affirmation conjoined with evidence. The refutation proceeds in several steps. First, it is argued that affirmation without evidence is not knowledge because knowledge must have a cause, either necessary or inferential. Since this affirmation has neither, it is not knowledge, and one who lacks knowledge of the world's origination, the existence and oneness of the Creator, and prophethood cannot be a believer. Second, the author provides a linguistic analysis of the word 'iman' (faith), showing that it derives from the root 'amn' (safety), meaning to bring oneself into safety by reflecting on evidence and being convinced of the truthfulness of the informant. Thus, bare affirmation is not faith. Third, after establishing that faith requires evidence, the author quotes Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila who insist that the evidence must be rational (aqli). They argue that the Prophet's statement cannot be used as evidence for the world's creation or the existence of the Creator because that would be circular: the Prophet's truthfulness is established only after knowing the Creator, and knowledge of the Creator requires prior knowledge of the world's creation. Therefore, one must first acquire rational knowledge of these matters. The passage concludes with an argument that the rational person must know that the Creator is wise and powerful to avoid the possibility of miracles being performed by false prophets, which would imply incapability on God's part.

Key Terms
التصديق
الإيمان
العلم
الضروري
الاستدلالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simply affirming with the heart is sufficient for faith.
Correction: The author shows that faith requires knowledge-based evidence and rational proof; bare affirmation is not faith.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is affirmation without evidence not considered knowledge?
A) Because it has no cause, either necessary or inferential
B) Because it is not accompanied by action
C) Because it contradicts the Prophet's teaching
D) Because it is based on tradition

Key Takeaway
Faith is not mere affirmation but affirmation grounded in evidence, as shown by logical and linguistic analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever honestly examined the evidence for your belief in Allah, or have you relied on inherited affirmation? If your faith is not built on evidence, what does this lesson say about the reality of your iman?
?
When you affirm 'I believe in Allah and His Messenger,' do you do so with the care of someone entering safety after reflection, or is it a hollow phrase? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen the evidential foundation of your faith?
?
The author says bare affirmation without evidence is not faith. If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He asked you for the basis of your iman, could you point to genuine reflection and knowledge, or only to habit? What will you change starting today?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

Evidence Must Be Rational: The Circularity Argument
Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila argue that the evidence for God's existence must be rational, not scriptural, due to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila insist that evidence for God's existence must be rational, due to the circularity of using prophetic statements.

Core Idea
Rational evidence is foundational; scriptural proof for God is circular.

After establishing that faith requires evidence, Nasafi turns to the nature of that evidence. Quoting Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila, he argues that the evidence must be rational (aqli). The reason is that using the Prophet's statement as evidence for God's existence is circular: one must first know God to verify prophethood, and knowing God requires knowledge of the world's creation. This passage highlights the priority of reason in theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the opening sentence: Al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila assert that evidence must be rational. Identify why scriptural evidence is insufficient.
This is a direct quote of their position.
2
Trace the circularity argument
Follow the logical chain: Prophetic statement → requires proof of prophethood → requires knowledge of the sender (God) → requires knowledge of the world's creation. See how this creates a circle.
The argument shows that one cannot prove God from scripture without already knowing God.
3
Examine the final point about miracles
The passage concludes that the rational person must know God's wisdom and power to avoid miracles being attributed to false prophets. This ties rational knowledge to the reliability of miracles.
This point reinforces the need for prior rational knowledge.
4
Summarize the reason for rational evidence
In your own words, explain why rational evidence is necessary before accepting any prophetic claim.
This connects to the broader theme of the priority of reason in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما من يقول : إنما اشترطنا اقتران الدليل بالتصديق ليصير الاعتقاد بدونه مبنيا على الدليل إيمانا، فيحتاج إلى التكلم معهم فنقول : بأنهم يقولون : إن التصديق إذا لم يقترن به دليل، يكون مجرد اعتقاد نشأ لا عن دليل، ومثل هذا الاعتقاد لا يكون علما ، إذ العلم المحدث لابد له من سبب ضروريا كان أو استدلاليا، ولا سبب لهذا الاعتقاد، فلم يكن علما، ولأن العلم المحدث نوعان ضروري واستدلالي لا ثالث لهما، وهذا الاعتقاد لم يثبت عن ضرورة فلم يكن ضروريا، ولا عن دليل فلم يكن استدلاليا ، فلم يكن علما، ومن المحال أن يكون من لا علم له بحدوث العالم، وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته وثبوت الرسالة مؤمنا، وهذا لأن التصديق وإن وجد إلا أن مطلق التصديق ليس بإيمان بل الإيمان هو التصديق المقيد بكونه مبنيًا على الدليل، وهذا لأن الإيمان في حقيقة اللغة ليس هو التصديق بل هو إدخال النفس في الأمان يقال: أمنته فأمن كما يقال : أجلسته فجلس ، فكل من أخبر بخبر فتأمل في دليله فعرف بالدليل صدقه فصدقه يقال : آمنه أي صدقه بعدما أدخل نفسه بالتأمل في دلائل صدقه في الأمان، من غير أن يكون مكذوبا أو مخدوعا أو ملبسا عليه في هذا الخبر، فإذا كان الأمر على هذا تبين أن التصديق العاري عن الدليل ليس بإيمان، وإذا ثبت هذا فبعد ذلك يقول أبو الحسن الأشعري والمعتزلة : إن الدليل لابد من أن يكون عقليا، إذ لا وجه إلى جعل قول الرسول، دليل حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع لأن قول الرسول لا يكون حجة ما لم يثبت رسالته، ولا وجه إلى القول برسالته إلا بعد معرفة مرسله ولن يتهيأ معرفة مرسله إلا بعد ثبوت المعرفة بحدوث العالم وإذا كان كذلك لن يتصور حصول المعرفة بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع بقول الرسول لأن المعرفة بصحة قوله مترتبة على معرفة حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع يحققه أن العاقل ما لم يعرف أن صانع العالم حكيم لا يظهر المعجزة على يد المتنبي، كما يذهب إليه القائلون بأن السفه ما خلا عن العاقبة الحميدة. أو يعرف أن صانع العالم تام القدرة يستحيل عليه العجز ، وظهور المعجزة على يد المتنبي، يعجزه عن

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the position of those who require evidence for faith itself. The author begins by stating that such opponents claim that faith is affirmation conjoined with evidence. The refutation proceeds in several steps. First, it is argued that affirmation without evidence is not knowledge because knowledge must have a cause, either necessary or inferential. Since this affirmation has neither, it is not knowledge, and one who lacks knowledge of the world's origination, the existence and oneness of the Creator, and prophethood cannot be a believer. Second, the author provides a linguistic analysis of the word 'iman' (faith), showing that it derives from the root 'amn' (safety), meaning to bring oneself into safety by reflecting on evidence and being convinced of the truthfulness of the informant. Thus, bare affirmation is not faith. Third, after establishing that faith requires evidence, the author quotes Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila who insist that the evidence must be rational (aqli). They argue that the Prophet's statement cannot be used as evidence for the world's creation or the existence of the Creator because that would be circular: the Prophet's truthfulness is established only after knowing the Creator, and knowledge of the Creator requires prior knowledge of the world's creation. Therefore, one must first acquire rational knowledge of these matters. The passage concludes with an argument that the rational person must know that the Creator is wise and powerful to avoid the possibility of miracles being performed by false prophets, which would imply incapability on God's part.

?

?

?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
6 min
Faith Does Not Require Prior Rational Proof
Al-Nasafi argues from the practice of the Prophet and caliphs that faith is valid without prior rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam al-Nasafi, rational proof is not a necessary condition for the validity of faith, based on the practice of the Prophet and the Rightly Guided Caliphs.

Core Idea
Faith does not require rational proof; the Prophet and caliphs accepted faith without it.

Imam al-Nasafi (d. 508/1114) was a leading Maturidi theologian. In this passage from 'Tabsirat al-Adilla', he argues against those who claim that rational proof is necessary for faith. He grounds his argument in the actual practice of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and the first caliphs, showing that they accepted people's faith without teaching them rational arguments. This lesson examines that argument and its implications for theology and practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the flow: al-Nasafi begins with a scenario of a new believer, then gives examples from the Prophet and caliphs, then presents a dilemma, and concludes.
The passage is dense with examples; take your time.
2
Identify the key examples
Find the examples: the initial convert who believed instantly, the Prophet not teaching him rational proofs, Abu Bakr accepting apostates without proof, Umar accepting peasants, and villagers/Magians entering Islam based on tawatur of miracles.
3
Understand the dilemma
Al-Nasafi sets up a dilemma: if rational proof were a condition, the Prophet and caliphs would have had to either reject these faiths or teach everyone kalam. Since they did neither, the condition is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the opponent's view contradicts the practice of the Prophet, caliphs, and all imams. Therefore, rational proof is not a condition for faith.
Consider the weight of prophetic practice in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لإخباره بوحدانية الله تعالى وخلع الأنداد، وأعرض عما كان يعتقده من ألوهية الأوثان والأصنام، وآمن بالبعث والنشور لمن في القبور من غير امتداد زمان يتمكن فيه من إحالة الرؤية والرجوع (١) إلى قصة العقل بالتأمل، بل في أسرع من لمح البصر، وكان الرسول بعده مؤمنا موقنا ولا يشتغل بتعليمه في الدلائل العقلية في كل مسألة من المسائل الاعتقادية مقدار ما يناظر الخصوم ويذب عن حريم الدين والملة، ويقدر على حل ما يرد عليه من الإشكال ولا يتعلم كيفية تركيب القياسات العقلية وطريق الإلزام والالتزام وآداب النظر والجدل. وكذا الخلفاء الراشدون - رضي الله عليهم أجمعين - فإن الصديق قبل إيمان من آمن من أهل الردة من غير الاشتغال بتعليمه إياهم من الدلائل ما يصيرون به مستنصرين من طريق العقل. وكذا عمر لما فتح سواد العراق قبل هو وعماله إيمان من كان بها من الرط والأنباط مع قلة أذهانهم، وبلادة أفهامهم، وتزجية عمرهم في الفلاحة وعمارة الأراضي وكري الأنهار. وكذا إيمان غيرهم من أهل القرى والرساتيق من المجوس وغيرهم مع معرفتهم بخلوهم عن صناعة الاستدلال، لما أنهم رأوهم قد صدقوا رسولهم بعدما بلغهم بطريق التواتر ظهور المعجزات على يده، ولو لم يكن ذلك إيمانا لفقد شرطه وهو الاستدلال العقلي بأحد أمرين: إما بالإعراض عن قبول إسلامهم وحكموا ببقائهم على ما كانوا عليه من الكفر، وضربوا الجزية على جماجمهم، والخراج على أراضيهم وعاملوهم معاملة أهل الذمة. وإما بنصب متكلم حاذق بصير بالأدلة، عالم بكيفية المحاجة، ليعلمهم جميعا صناعة الكلام، وعند امتناعهم وامتناع كل من الأئمة في البلاد أجمع إلى يومنا هذا عن ذلك وتركهم جميع العوام يشتغلون بالمكاسب، ولا يتعلمون الكلام ظهر أن ما ذهب إليه الخصوم خلاف صنع رسول الله وخلفائه وأئمة أمته وجميع المسلمين، وما هذا سبيله فهو باطل، والله الموفق.
Because of his informing of the oneness of God Most High and the rejection of associates, and he turned away from what he used to believe of the divinity of idols and images, and he believed in the resurrection and raising of those in graves without any lapse of time in which he could transform the vision and return to the story of reason by reflection, but rather in the twinkling of an eye, and the Messenger after him was a believer, certain, and he did not occupy himself with teaching him rational proofs in every issue of the doctrinal matters to the extent that he would argue with opponents and defend the sanctity of religion and the community, and he could resolve what difficulties came to him without learning how to construct rational syllogisms, the method of imposing and conceding, and the etiquette of debate and argumentation. Likewise the Rightly Guided Caliphs—may God be pleased with them all—for Al-Siddiq accepted the faith of those who believed from the people of apostasy without occupying himself with teaching them proofs by which they would become helpers from the path of reason. And likewise 'Umar, when he conquered the Sawad of Iraq, he and his officials accepted the faith of those among them who were peasants and Nabataeans, despite their weak intellects, their stupidity, and their spending their lives in agriculture, land cultivation, and digging canals. And likewise the faith of others from the villagers and rural areas among the Magians and others, despite their known lack of the art of inference, because they saw that they had believed their messenger after it reached them through tawatur (concurrent transmission) the appearance of miracles at his hand. And if that were not faith because of lacking its condition, which is rational inference, then [the Prophet and caliphs would be forced into] one of two matters: either by turning away from accepting their Islam and judging them to remain on what they were of disbelief, and imposing jizya on their heads, and kharaj on their lands, and treating them as dhimmis; or by appointing a skilled mutakallim, insightful in proofs, knowledgeable in the method of argumentation, to teach all of them the art of kalam. And given their refraining and the refraining of every one of the imams in all lands to this day from that, and their leaving all common people to occupy themselves with earning a livelihood without learning kalam, it becomes clear that what the opponents have adopted contradicts the practice of the Messenger of God, his caliphs, the imams of his community, and all Muslims, and whatever is on this path is false. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a proof-by-practice argument showing that faith is valid without prior rational proof. The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, begins by describing how a new believer comes to faith: through hearing the Prophet's message of God's oneness and rejection of idols, he turns from polytheism and believes in resurrection, all occurring instantly, without extended rational deliberation. The Prophet, after his conversion, does not spend time teaching him rational proofs for each creedal issue; instead, the believer learns only enough to defend the faith and engage opponents. Then al-Nasafi gives examples from the Rightly Guided Caliphs: Abu Bakr accepted the faith of the apostates (ahl al-ridda) without teaching them rational proofs. Umar accepted the faith of the peasants and Nabataeans of Iraq despite their intellectual weakness. Similarly, villagers and Magians entered Islam without any training in the art of inference (sina'at al-istidlal); they simply relied on the tawatur (widespread transmission) of the Prophet's miracles. Al-Nasafi then sets up a dilemma: if faith required rational inference, then the Prophet and caliphs would have had to do one of two things: either reject these people's faith (which they did not) or appoint a skilled mutakallim to teach them kalam (which they did not). Since the Prophet and all subsequent imams have refrained from teaching kalam to the masses and instead let them pursue livelihoods, it is clear that rational proof is not a condition for faith. Therefore, the opposing view (that rational proof is necessary) contradicts the practice of the Prophet, his caliphs, and the entire Muslim community, and is thus false.

Key Terms
إيمان (faith)
استدلال عقلي (rational proof)
صناعة الكلام (art of kalam)
تواتر (tawatur)
متكلم (mutakallim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is invalid unless one has studied rational theology (kalam) and can provide rational proofs.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the Prophet and caliphs accepted faith from people who had no such training, so rational proof is not a necessary condition for valid faith.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what does the practice of the Prophet and the Rightly Guided Caliphs demonstrate about the requirement of rational proof for faith?
A) Rational proof is a necessary condition for valid faith.
B) Rational proof is not a necessary condition; faith can be valid without it.
C) Rational proof is only required for scholars, not common people.
D) The Prophet required all converts to learn rational proofs before accepting their faith.

Key Takeaway
The practice of the Prophet and the caliphs demonstrates that rational proof is not a condition for the validity of faith; faith can be valid when based on acceptance of prophetic message and miraculous signs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you first believed, did you rely more on rational arguments or on the simple acceptance of the Prophet's message? Are you now trusting your intellect more than your submission?
?
Have you ever looked down on someone whose faith seemed 'simple' or lacking in theological depth, thinking they were less Muslim? How does al-Nasafi's argument challenge that attitude?
?
If you were asked today to justify your faith, would you rush to rational proofs or simply say 'I believe because the Prophet brought the truth'? What does your instinct reveal about the state of your heart?

Iman as Tasdiq: The Sunni Position and Refutation of the Linguistic Objection
Affirmation of iman as tasdiq and refutation of the linguistic objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sunni scholars define iman as tasdiq (assent) and how they refute the objection that iman requires entering security.

Core Idea
Iman is simply assent (tasdiq), not entering security.

This passage presents the Sunni consensus that iman is tasdiq, and then tackles a linguistic objection that argues iman means entering security. The author uses both a rational argument and a grammatical point from Arabic usage to prove that iman is inherently tasdiq, independent of any sense of security. Understanding this refutation is key to grasping the Sunni doctrine of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice that the author starts by stating the Sunni position and then addresses an objection.
The passage ends mid-sentence but the argument is complete enough to understand the main point.
2
Identify the Sunni claim and the objection
The Sunni claim is that iman is tasdiq, and the person who believes a miracle-supported report has indeed believed. The objection says that this tasdiq is not iman because it does not involve entering security (amān).
The objection is based on a supposed linguistic derivation of 'iman' from 'amān'.
3
Follow the linguistic argument
The author argues that 'iman' is not derived from 'amān'; rather, it is simply tasdiq. He gives evidence from common Arabic usage: people say 'āmana bihi' to mean 'he believed him' even when there is no element of security.
Think of examples like 'āmana bi-llāh' – no one thinks it means entering God's security.
4
Understand the grammatical point
The author uses a powerful grammatical argument: if 'āmana' meant bringing oneself into security, the verb would be reflexive (āmana nafsī), but it is used transitively with 'bi' or 'li' to relate to the informer.
This shows that the action is directed toward the informer (assenting to him), not toward oneself.
5
Reflect on the theological importance
This distinction matters because if iman required entering security, then belief in unseen matters (like prophethood) would be invalid until one is completely secure. But iman is simply assent of the heart, which can be given even with some doubt or risk.
This protects the Sunni definition of iman as tasdiq only, without additional conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عامة أهل السنة والجماعة من الفقهاء والمتكلمين فإنهم يقولون: أن هذا الرجل مأمور بالإيمان وقد آمن إذ الإيمان هو التصديق، وقد وجد منه التصديق، فينال الثواب الموعود إذ الثواب ينال بفضل الله فيناله من وعد له به سواء لحقته المشقة أو لم تلحقه. وقولهم: إن هذا التصديق ليس بإيمان لأن إدخال النفس في الأمان من أن يكون مكذوبا أو مخدوعا أو ملبسا عليه لم يوجد هذا باطل، لأن الإيمان في اللغة عبارة عن التصديق من غير أن يكون مأخوذا من الأمان يقال: آمن به وآمن له أي صدقه، فإن واحدا من عرض الناس لو أخبر بخبر محتمل في العقل لا وجوب فيه ولا امتناع فصدقه من لا عهد له به بالمخبر فيما مضى من الأزمنة، لم يمتنع من أهل اللغة أن يقول: آمن له وإن لم يعرف دليل صحته وإن لم يدخل نفسه في الأمان بل يطلقون ذلك لوجود نفس التصديق منه إياه، يدل عليه أنك تقول: آمنت لفلان أو آمنت به ولو كان هذا إدخال النفس في الأمان لكان لا تعلق لهذا الفعل بالمخبر بل تعلقه بنفس السامع، لأنه أدخل نفسه في الأمان لا المخبر، فينبغي أن يقول: آمنت نفسي لا أن يقول: آمنت بفلان، وإذا قيل: آمنت به دل أنه
As for the generality of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a among the jurists and theologians, they say: This man is commanded to believe and he has believed, for iman is tasdiq (assent), and tasdiq has occurred from him, so he attains the promised reward, for reward is attained by Allah's grace, so he attains it whom He has promised it, whether hardship befell him or not. Their saying: 'This tasdiq is not iman because bringing oneself into security (amān) from being lied to, deceived, or confused has not occurred' is false. For iman linguistically is an expression of tasdiq, not being derived from amān. It is said: 'āmana bihi' and 'āmana lahu' meaning he believed him. For if one ordinary person informed another of a report that is rationally possible, neither necessary nor impossible, and he believed him, even if he had no previous acquaintance with the informer, it is not prevented for the people of language to say 'āmana lahu', even if he does not know the proof of its correctness and does not bring himself into security. Rather, they utter that for the very occurrence of tasdiq from him. This is evidenced by the fact that you say: 'āmana li-fulān' or 'āmana bihi'. If this were bringing oneself into security, then this action would have no relation to the informer, but rather it would be related to the hearer himself, because he brought himself into security, not the informer. So he should say: 'āmana nafsī' (I gave myself security), not 'āmana bi-fulān'. And when it is said 'āmana bihi', it indicates that
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni position on the man who believes based on a miracle-supported report. The author affirms that such a person is commanded to believe and has indeed believed, because iman is tasdiq, which has occurred. Therefore, he merits the promised reward by Allah's grace, whether or not he experienced hardship in believing. The passage then refutes the objection that this tasdiq is not iman because it does not involve entering into security (amān) from being deceived. The author argues that iman linguistically is tasdiq, not derived from amān. He provides evidence from common linguistic usage: people say 'āmana bihi' to mean 'he believed him' even without knowing proof or entering into security. He uses a grammatical argument: if it meant bringing oneself into security, the verb would be reflexive (āmana nafsī) rather than transitive with bi- or li-. The passage ends mid-sentence but the argument is clear.

Correction: The author shows that iman is simply tasdiq (assent) in Arabic usage, not derived from amān. The verb 'āmana' is used transitively for assent, not reflexively for security.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The author argues that iman is simply tasdiq, not entering security. Does your worship depend on feeling secure in your faith, or on the reality of your assent to Allah? What would change if you focused on tasdiq rather than security?
?
If iman is tasdiq, then your faith is measured by the truth of what you affirm, not by your emotional state. Are you willing to examine the content of your tasdiq—what you truly assent to—even if it is uncomfortable?

The Linguistic Meaning of Iman: Tashdiq, Not Security
The author states the Sunni definition of iman and refutes a linguistic objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni scholars define iman as tasdiq and how linguistic analysis refutes the claim that iman derives from aman (security).

Core Idea
Iman means affirmation (tashdiq), not security; Arabic usage proves it.

In this passage, the author defends the Sunni definition of faith as tasdiq against a linguistic objection. Some argued that iman must mean 'entering into security' because it shares a root with aman. The author counters by analyzing how the verb āmana is actually used in Arabic. This lesson unpacks both the theological position and the linguistic evidence, showing how careful attention to language clarifies doctrinal truth.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Sunni position
Read the opening sentence: the Sunni consensus that a person who affirms the prophet truly has iman and deserves reward, regardless of hardship.
Notice the connection between tasdiq and reward.
2
Examine the objection and refutation
The author rejects the claim that tasdiq without 'entering into security' is not iman. Identify the linguistic evidence: verbs āmana bihi/āmana lahu mean 'affirmed him', not 'gave security'.
Pay attention to the hypothetical example.
3
Analyze the grammatical argument
The author argues that if iman meant 'entering into security', it would be reflexive, but it's transitive to the object of belief. This proves it is tasdiq.
Consider how grammar supports theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عامة أهل السنة والجماعة من الفقهاء والمتكلمين فإنهم يقولون : أن هذا الرجل مأمور بالإيمان وقد آمن إذ الإيمان هو التصديق، وقد وجد منه التصديق، فينال الثواب الموعود إذ الثواب ينال بفضل الله فيناله من وعد له به سواء لحقته المشقة أو لم تلحقه. وقولهم : إن هذا التصديق ليس بإيمان لأن إدخال النفس في الأمان من أن يكون مكذوبا أو مخدوعا أو ملبسا عليه لم يوجد هذا باطل، لأن الإيمان في اللغة عبارة عن التصديق من غير أن يكون مأخوذا من الأمان يقال : آمن به وآمن له أي صدقه، فإن واحدا من عرض الناس لو أخبر بخبر محتمل في العقل لا وجوب فيه ولا امتناع فصدقه من لا عهد له به بالمخبر فيما مضى من الأزمنة، لم يمتنع من أهل اللغة أن يقول : آمن له وإن لم يعرف دليل صحته وإن لم يدخل نفسه في الأمان بل يطلقون ذلك لوجود نفس التصديق منه إياه، يدل عليه أنك تقول: آمنت لفلان أو آمنت به ولو كان هذا إدخال النفس في الأمان لكان لا تعلق لهذا الفعل بالمخبر ، بل تعلقه بنفس السامع، لأنه أدخل نفسه في الأمان لا المخبر، فينبغي أن يقول: آمنت نفسي لا أن يقول : آمنت بفلان، وإذا قيل: آمنت به دل أنه عبارة عن التصديق فإنه المتعلق بالمخبر، إلا أنه يتعدى إلى المفعول بدون حرف العلة ولفظ الإيمان يتعدى إلى المفعول بالصلة، وكم من فعل تعديه العرب بالصلات، وإن كان يجوز في الأصل التعدية بدون الحرف، يقال: شكرت لفلان ونصحت له، فإذا كان الأمر كذلك بطل هذا الكلام.
As for the general body of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a from the jurists and theologians, they say: This man is commanded to have faith (iman) and he has believed, for iman is tashdiq (belief/affirmation), and tashdiq has occurred from him, so he attains the promised reward (thawab), since reward is attained by Allah's grace, so he attains it whom Allah has promised it, whether hardship befell him or not. And their statement: 'This tashdiq is not iman because entering oneself into security (aman) from being a liar or deceived or deceived about is not present' is false, because iman in the language (lugha) is speech about tashdiq, not derived from aman. It is said: 'amana bihi' and 'amana lahu' meaning he affirmed him. For if one of the common people were to inform of something intellectually possible, neither necessary nor impossible, and someone who has no prior acquaintance with the informer concerning past times affirmed him, it would not be impossible for the people of language to say: 'amana lahu' even if he does not know the proof of its correctness and even if he does not enter himself into security. Rather they use that because of the mere occurrence of tashdiq from him towards him. Evidence for this is that you say: 'amantu li-fulan' or 'amantu bihi'. If this were entering oneself into security, then this act would have no relation to the informer, but rather its relation would be to the hearer himself, because he enters himself into security, not the informer. So he would need to say: 'amantu nafsi' (I have given myself security), not 'amantu bi-fulan' (I have believed in so-and-so). And if one says: 'amantu bihi', it indicates that it is speech about tashdiq, for it is related to the informer, except that it is transitive to the object without a particle, while the word iman is transitive to the object with a connector. And many verbs are made transitive by the Arabs with connectors, even though original transitive without a particle is permissible. One says: 'shakartu li-fulan' and 'nasahtu lahu'. So if this is the case, this statement is invalid.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni view and uses linguistic analysis to defend it. The author first states the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna: the man who believes the prophet is commanded to have iman, he has iman because iman is tashdiq, and tashdiq has occurred from him. Therefore he receives the promised reward, by Allah's grace, regardless of any hardship. Then the author refutes an objection: some claim that this tashdiq is not iman because it lacks 'entering oneself into security' (from being deceived). The author rejects this etymologically, arguing that iman in Arabic means tashdiq, not aman. He provides linguistic evidence: the verb 'amana bihi/amana lahu' means 'affirmed him'. He gives a hypothetical: if a common person informs of a plausible matter and another affirms him despite no prior knowledge, native speakers would still say 'amana lahu' even if the hearer has no proof and does not enter into security. The author further argues that if iman meant entering into security, the verb would be intransitive or reflexive ('amantu nafsi'), but it is transitive to the object of belief, showing it is tashdiq. He concludes that the objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Iman in its essential linguistic meaning is tasdiq (affirmation), not aman (security), as proven by Arabic usage and grammatical structure.

The author argues that iman in Arabic means affirmation even when you lack proof or certainty. Does your own iman rest on a simple, unquestioned affirmation of the truth of Islam, or do you demand intellectual proofs before you affirm? What would it mean for your daily practice if you truly accepted that iman is just tashdiq—that you have already fulfilled its linguistic requirement?

Are you one of those who affirms with the tongue but resists with actions? Refutation of Ash'ari's Intermediate State
Maturidi refutation of Ash'ari's intermediate state between faith and disbelief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi tradition rejects the Ash'ari position that a person with tasdiq but no istidlal occupies an intermediate state between faith and disbelief.

Core Idea
Ash'ari's intermediate position is internally contradictory and leads to absurd consequences.

This passage presents the Maturidi critique of Ash'ari's claim that a person who assents (tasdiq) but does not engage in rational inference (istidlal) is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. The author argues that this view is linguistically and logically flawed, and that it inadvertently adopts the Mu'tazilite doctrine of 'al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' which Ash'ari himself rejects. By analyzing Ash'ari's own statements, the Maturidi shows that his position is self-contradictory and leads to impossible conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Examine the linguistic argument
Read how the author uses the transitive nature of 'tasdiq' versus 'iman' to question Ash'ari's distinction. Note that Arabic verbs can take prepositions without changing meaning.
2
Identify the logical contradiction
Focus on the claim that Ash'ari removes the person from kufr but does not enter him into iman. Ask: if tasdiq repels kufr, why isn't it iman?
3
Compare with Mu'tazila and fasiq
See how the author draws a parallel between Ash'ari's position and the Mu'tazilite 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn', and then examines Ash'ari's treatment of the person as a fasiq.
4
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
Read the final part where the author shows that if tasdiq is not iman, it becomes takdhib, which is absurd. This demonstrates the impossibility of Ash'ari's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبارة عن التصديق فإنه المتعلق بالمخبر، إلا أنه يتعدى إلى المفعول بدون حرف العلة ولفظ الإيمان يتعدى إلى المفعول بالصلة، وكم من فعل تعديه العرب بالصلات، وإن كان يجوز في الأصل التعدية بدون الحرف، يقال: شكرت لفلان ونصحت له، فإذا كان الأمر كذلك بطل هذا الكلام، والذي يدل على بطلانه على أصل الأشعري أن عنده يخرج به عن الكفر واسطة بين الكفر والإيمان ولو لم يكن الإيمان موجودا لما اندفع به الكفر، ثم هذا من الأشعري مناقضة ظاهرة، حيث أخرجه بهذا من الكفر وما أدخله به في الإيمان، وهذا منه إثبات المنزلة بين المنزلتين، كما فعلت المعتزلة وهو يأبى ذلك، ثم العجب منه أن يجعله بمنزلة فساق أهل الملة حيث قال: يعذب بقدر ذنبه وهو ترك الاستدلال - ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة، ولو عفى عنه فأدخله الجنة ابتداء جاز، وهذا هو حكم الفساق، فإن كان فاسقا لتركه النظر، لماذا لم يحكم بإسلامه والكبيرة عندنا لا تنافي الإسلام؟ وإن يكن فعله إيمانا لماذا لم يبق على الكفر، وهو لا ينفي ألا يوجد الإيمان، فإثبات المنزلة بين المنزلتين من أين؟ ثم كما أن المعتزلة اخطأوا حيث أثبتوا الخلود في النيران لمن ليس بكافر فكذا الأشعري أخطأ حيث أدخل الجنة من ليس بمؤمن، يحققه أن الثواب يعرف بوعد الله تعالى والله تعالى ما وعد الجنة إلا للمؤمنين فبأي دليل يقول بدخول من ليس بمؤمن في الجنة؟ ولو قال: أنا لا أقول بأنه مؤمن وأنه كافر وهو يخلد في النار فيقال له: كيف يكون كافرا وقد تبدل منه التكذيب بالتصديق وأنت تقول: إن الإيمان هو التصديق، والكفر هو التكذيب؟ فإن لم يجعل تصديقه إيمانا لأنه لم يدخل نفسه في الأمان فلماذا جعلت تصديقه الثابت في الحقيقة تكذيبا؟ وهل هذا إلا كجعل السكون حركة، والسواد بياضا؟ وكل ذلك مستحيل متناقض والله الموفق.
It is an expression of tasdiq (assent), for it is related to the informant, except that it is transitive to the object without a particle of cause, while the word iman is transitive to the object with a particle. And many verbs are made transitive by the Arabs with particles, even though originally it is permissible to make them transitive without the particle. It is said: 'I thanked so-and-so' (shakartu li-fulan) and 'I advised him' (nasaltu lahu). So when this is the case, this statement is invalid. And what indicates its invalidity according to Ash'ari's principle is that with him (Ash'ari) a person departs by it (tasdiq) from kufr, but there is an intermediary between kufr and iman. If iman did not exist, kufr would not be repelled by it. Then this from Ash'ari is an apparent contradiction: he removed him by this from kufr but did not enter him thereby into iman. This is from him an affirmation of the station between the two stations (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), as the Mu'tazila did, while he rejects that. Then amazing from him is that he makes him like the sinful (fussaq) of the people of the religion, where he said: 'He is punished according to his sin, which is abandoning istidlal (inference), then his final outcome is paradise. And if He forgives him and enters him into paradise initially, it is permissible.' And this is the ruling for the sinful (fussaq). If he is a sinner (fasiq) for abandoning nazar (consideration), why is he not judged to be a Muslim, while the major sin (kabira) with us does not contradict Islam? And if his act is iman, why does he not remain upon kufr? And he does not negate that iman exists? So from where is the affirmation of the station between the two stations? Then, just as the Mu'tazila erred when they affirmed eternal stay in the Fire for those who are not disbelievers, likewise Ash'ari erred when he entered into paradise one who is not a believer. This is verified by the fact that reward is known by the promise of Allah Most High, and Allah Most High did not promise paradise except to the believers. So by what evidence does he say that a non-believer enters paradise? And if he says: 'I do not say that he is a believer nor that he is a disbeliever, and he will be eternally in the Fire,' it is said to him: 'How can he be a disbeliever while he has replaced takdhib (disbelief) with tasdiq, and you say that iman is tasdiq and kufr is takdhib?' If he does not make his tasdiq iman because he did not enter himself into safety (aman), then why did you make his tasdiq, established in reality, into takdhib? Is this not like making stillness a movement, and blackness whiteness? All of that is impossible and contradictory. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari position on faith. The author begins by noting that the term 'iman' is linguistically used with a preposition (bi) while 'tasdiq' is transitive without a preposition, but Arabic verbs often take prepositions. He then states that Ash'ari's claim that a person who has tasdiq but does not engage in istidlal is neither a believer nor a disbeliever but in an intermediate station is contradictory. He argues that Ash'ari's own principle should make such a person a believer because the tasdiq removes him from kufr. If he is not a believer, then kufr would not be repelled, but Ash'ari says he is not a kafir. This is a contradiction. The author accuses Ash'ari of adopting the Mu'tazilite doctrine of manzila bayn al-manzilatayn, which Ash'ari rejects. Moreover, Ash'ari treats such a person like a fasiq (grave sinner) from the Muslim community, punishing him for his sin (abandoning istidlal) but eventually admitting him to paradise. The author questions why, if he is a fasiq for abandoning nazar, he is not considered a Muslim (since major sin does not negate Islam in Maturidi view). And if his act (tasdiq) is iman, why does he remain in kufr? The author concludes that the Ash'ari position is self-contradictory. He further argues that God only promised paradise to believers, so how can Ash'ari promise it to a non-believer? If Ash'ari says the person is a kafir, then how can his tasdiq not be considered takdhib (disbelief)? The author likens this to claiming stillness is motion or blackness is whiteness, which is absurd.

Key Terms
تصديق
إيمان
كفر
منزلة بين المنزلتين
فساق
استدلال
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'ari's position that a person with tasdiq but no istidlal is in an intermediate state between faith and disbelief is a coherent third option.
Correction: The Maturidi author demonstrates that this position is logically contradictory: if tasdiq repels kufr, it must be iman; otherwise, repelling kufr is impossible. The intermediate state collapses into absurdity.

Checkpoint

B) The position of the Khawarij on takfir
C) The position of the Jahmiyya on irja'
D) The position of the Shi'a on imama

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari's intermediate state between iman and kufr is impossible because tasdiq either is iman (repelling kufr) or is not; both options lead to contradictions with Ash'ari's own definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author shows that holding two contradictory positions at once leads to absurdity. In your own life, are there areas where you try to maintain a comfortable middle ground between clear obligations and your own desires? What inconsistency are you unwilling to confront?
?
This lesson argues that tasdiq either makes you a believer or it does not—there is no safe third option. Do you live with the full commitment of faith, or do you hold back, hoping that a little assent will keep you safe without requiring full submission? What would change if you acted on your tasdiq as complete iman?
?
Allah only promised paradise to believers. Are you certain that you are among them, or do you secretly rely on a 'middle state' that this passage proves impossible? What practical step can you take today to move from mere assent to true iman that enters you into safety?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite View on the Major Sinner and the Person with Doubts
Refutation of Mu'tazila's view on status of fasiq and shubha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi author argues that the Mu'tazilite position on the committer of a major sin and the person facing doubts is internally inconsistent.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites' own principles contradict their conclusions about faith and sin.

In this passage, the Maturidi theologian critiques the Mu'tazilite stance on two groups: the committer of a major sin (fasiq) and the person grappling with a doubt (shubha). He shows that their classification leads to contradictions. Let's read the argument and see why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's strong language: 'bāṭil aydan' (also invalid).
2
Identify the two cases discussed
The passage addresses two types of people: the committer of a major sin (sāḥib al-kabīra) and the one who faces a doubt (man tawajjahat ʿalayhi shubha). Find where each is mentioned.
The author treats them separately, pointing out problems with the Mu'tazilite treatment of each.
3
Analyze the contradiction for the major sinner
The author says the major sinner has tasdiq that brings him into safety (amān), fulfilling conditions of iman, yet Mu'tazila deny him the title of believer. Identify why this is contradictory.
If tasdiq is present and conditions met, consistency would require calling him a believer.
4
Analyze the case of the person with doubt
For the person seeking to repel a doubt, the author says there is no guarantee of success. Until success, the truth of his creed is not apparent. But if his belief remains undisturbed, he remains a believer. See how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.
The Mu'tazila likely considered such a person as not fully believing, but the author insists on preserving iman as long as conviction is steady.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by declaring the Mu'tazilite school inconsistent and self-contradictory. Consider how this critique fits within the larger Maturidi project of defending orthodox faith.

وهذا الكلام على أصول المعتزلة باطل أيضا، لأن صاحب الكبيرة وجد منه التصديق على وجه أدخل نفسه في الأمان، وتحقق بحده وشرطه وهو مع هذا ليس بمؤمن ومن توجهت عليه شبهة لم يتمكن من دفعها بعد وهو في طلب وجه الدفع، فلا أمان له من انعدام الظفر بما يتمكن به من دفعها وما لم يتمكن من ذلك لا يظهر أن ما ذهب إليه من العقيدة صحيح إذ الحق ما غلبت حُجَجه وأظهر أسباب التمويه في غيره، وإذا لم يظهر ذلك لم يعرف أنه لم يكن مكذوبا فيما أخبر بصحة هذا إذا كان في طلب دفعها ولم يضطرب في اعتقاده بقي مؤمنا، دل ومع أن هذا الكلام فاسد ولا استمرار لمذهبهم بل هي مذاهب متناقضة، ينقض البعض البعض، والحمد لله الذي هدانا لدينه القويم وصراطه المستقيم.
This statement is also invalid according to the principles of the Mu'tazila, because the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has manifested tasdiq in a way that brings himself into safety (aman), and it is realized by its definition and condition, yet with all this he is not a believer. And one who is faced with a doubt (shubha) and is not yet able to repel it while he is seeking a way to repel it, then there is no safety for him from failure to attain what he can repel it with. And as long as he does not attain that, it is not apparent that what he holds of creed is correct, for the truth is that whose arguments prevail and the means of obfuscation in others are made apparent. And if that does not become apparent, it is not known that he is not lying in what he reports as truth. This is if he is seeking to repel the doubt and does not become disturbed in his belief; he remains a believer. Therefore, this statement is corrupt and there is no consistency to their school; rather they are contradictory schools, some refuting others. And praise be to Allah who guided us to His upright religion and straight path.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazilite position on the major sinner (fasiq) and the person facing doubts (shubha). The author argues that according to Mu'tazilite principles, a person who commits a major sin has tasdiq that brings him into 'aman' (safety) and fulfills the conditions of iman, yet they do not consider him a believer. This is inconsistent. Regarding the person facing a doubt, the author says that if he is actively seeking to repel the doubt, there is no guarantee he will succeed. Until he succeeds, it cannot be known that his belief is correct, because truth is known by its arguments prevailing. However, if his belief remains firm without disturbance, he remains a believer. The author concludes that the Mu'tazilite position is self-contradictory and inconsistent across their various schools. He ends with gratitude to Allah for guidance.

The Purpose of Tasdiq: Establishing the Rulings of Islam
Answer to the objection that tasdiq must be preceded by 'ilm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi response to the objection about tasdiq being based on knowledge focuses on the practical rulings (ahkam) of Islam rather than the mere label of iman.

Core Idea
The goal of faith is to secure the rulings of Islam, not just the name 'iman'.

In this passage, the author addresses the objection that true iman must be preceded by knowledge. He counters that even without knowledge, the simple act of tasdiq is sufficient to remove a person from kufr and entitle them to the promised reward. The Maturidi perspective shifts the focus from the label to the practical consequences: what matters is that the person is treated as a believer in this world and the next, not whether they can be called 'mu'min' in the fullest sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing too deeply. Then read the literal translation to get the gist. Note that the author is responding to an objection, so he begins with 'قلنا' (we say).
2
Identify the two parts of the response
The response has two main moves: first, an assertion that even without knowledge, tasdiq removes kufr and earns reward; second, a logical turn where the author says the opponent's own concession for establishing rulings can be used to justify the name.
3
Understand the key distinction
Notice the distinction between 'ahkam' (rulings) and 'ism' (name). The author argues that the name is pointless without the rulings, and the loss of the name is harmless if the rulings remain. This is central to the Maturidi position.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this argument is important for Maturidi theology. It shows that iman is defined by its effect (tasdiq) rather than by the means (knowledge). This allows a more inclusive understanding of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن التصديق ينبغي أن يكون عن علم ، قلنا: ومع هذا عند الأشعري بهذا التصديق، يخرج عن الكفر ويستحق الثواب الموعود للمؤمن، ونحن لا نروم إلا هذا، إذ غرضنا من هذا إثبات أحكام الإسلام، لا إطلاق العبارة إذ لا ينفع بإطلاق التسمية مع انعدام الأحكام، ولا ضرر بزوال التسمية مع بقاء أحكام الإسلام، ثم نقول له : كل عذر لك في إثبات الأحكام فهو عذرنا في إطلاق الاسم، يحققه أن الاستدلال وتحصيل العلم ليتوصل بهما إلى التصديق الذي هو المقصد
And their saying: 'Verily, tasdiq must be based on knowledge' – we say: Even so, according to Ash'ari, by this tasdiq, he leaves kufr and deserves the promised reward for the believer. We desire nothing but this, for our purpose in this is to establish the rulings (ahkam) of Islam, not to use the expression, for there is no benefit in applying the name while the rulings are absent, and there is no harm in the removal of the name while the rulings of Islam remain. Then we say to him: Every excuse you have for establishing the rulings is our excuse for applying the name. This is verified by the fact that istidlal (inference) and acquisition of knowledge are only to reach the tasdiq that is the intended goal.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection: that tasdiq must be based on 'ilm (knowledge) to count as iman. The author counters that even according to Ash'ari, this very tasdiq (even without 'ilm?) removes the person from kufr and qualifies him for the reward promised to believers. The author states that his aim is only to establish the rulings (ahkam) of Islam for such a person, not to insist on the label 'iman'. He argues that it is useless to have the name without the rulings, and no harm in losing the name while the rulings remain. Then he turns the argument: any excuse (concession) the opponent offers for establishing the rulings can be used by the Maturidi to apply the name. The reality is that istidlal and acquisition of knowledge are only means to reach the tasdiq, which is the ultimate goal. So if tasdiq is present, the goal is achieved, regardless of whether the means were employed.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tasdiq must be preceded by knowledge to be considered iman and have any effect.
Correction: Tasdiq alone, even without prior knowledge, is sufficient to remove kufr, establish the ahkam of Islam, and entitle one to reward.

Checkpoint

B) There is no benefit in applying the name when the rulings are absent

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq, even without prior knowledge, is sufficient to remove a person from kufr and entitle them to the rulings of Islam in this world and the reward in the next.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Absurdity of Denying Tasdiq without Aman
Initial reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazila claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that equating tasdiq without aman to kufr is a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Calling tasdiq without aman kufr is like calling stillness movement.

The author begins by exposing the absurdity of the Mu'tazila claim. He argues that if a person's tasdiq (assent) is genuine, it cannot be considered disbelief simply because it does not bring about 'aman' (security) in the Mu'tazila sense. This leads to a logical contradiction, as he demonstrates with a striking analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the rhetorical question and the analogy.
The author uses a strong analogy to highlight the absurdity.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The passage presupposes the Mu'tazila claim: tasdiq without aman is not faith but kufr. Look for how the author refutes this.
3
Analyze the analogy
The author compares the claim to 'making stillness movement'. Reflect on why this analogy is powerful.
Both are opposites; equating them is impossible.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that this position is 'impossible and contradictory'. This sets the stage for further refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصديقه إيمانا لأنه لم يدخل نفسه في الأمان فلماذا جعلت تصديقه الثابت في الحقيقة تكذيبا؟ وهل هذا إلا كجعل السكون حركة، والسواد بياضا؟ وكل ذلك مستحيل متناقض والله الموفق. وهذا الكلام على أصول المعتزلة باطل أيضا، لأن صاحب الكبيرة وجد منه التصديق على وجه أدخل نفسه في الأمان، وتحقق بحده وشرطه وهو مع هذا ليس بمؤمن ومن توجهت عليه شبهة لم يتمكن من دفعها بعد وهو في طلب وجه الدفع، فلا أمان له من انعدام الظفر بما يتمكن به من دفعها وما لم يتمكن من ذلك لا يظهر أن ما ذهب إليه من العقيدة صحيح إذ الحق ما غلبت حُجَجه وأظهر أسباب التمويه في غيره، وإذا لم يظهر ذلك لم يعرف أنه لم يكن مكذوبا فيما أخبر بصحة هذا إذا كان في طلب دفعها ولم يضطرب في اعتقاده بقي مؤمنا، دل ومع أن هذا الكلام فاسد ولا استمرار لمذهبهم بل هي مذاهب متناقضة، ينقض البعض البعض، والحمد لله الذي هدانا لدينه القويم وصراطه المستقيم.
His tasdiq (assent) is faith because he did not enter himself into security (aman). So why do you make his tasdiq, which is established in reality, tantamount to disbelief? Is this not like making stillness movement, and blackness whiteness? All of that is impossible and contradictory, and God is the Grantor of success. This statement is also false according to the principles of the Mu'tazila, because the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has tasdiq from him in a manner that entered him into security, and is realized by its definition and condition, yet he is not a believer according to them. Also, one who is confronted by an ambiguity (shubha) which he cannot yet repel, while he is seeking a means of repelling it, has no security from failure to attain that by which he can repel it. And if he cannot do that, it does not become apparent that what he holds of belief is correct, for the truth is that whose evidences overcome and whose [opponent's] means of falsification become manifest. If that does not become apparent, it is not known that he was not lying regarding what he affirmed of the soundness of this [belief], while he is seeking to repel it and is not perturbed in his conviction, he remains a believer. [The text says: 'dalla wa ma'a anna hadha al-kalam fasid'] And this statement is corrupt and there is no consistency to their school; rather they are contradictory schools, some contradicting others. Praise be to God who guided us to His upright religion and straight path.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazila argument about the relationship between tasdiq (assent) and aman (security) in defining faith. The author first presents the Mu'tazila claim: if someone's tasdiq does not bring them into a state of aman, then it is not true faith but rather kufr. The author rejects this as impossible, comparing it to turning stillness into movement, a logical contradiction. He then argues that even on Mu'tazila principles this claim is false. He gives two examples: first, the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has tasdiq that does bring him into aman according to their own definition, yet they do not call him a believer—a contradiction. Second, a person faced with an ambiguity (shubha) that he cannot yet repel, while sincerely seeking to refute it, remains a believer because his inability does not negate his conviction. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila position is internally inconsistent and their different opinions contradict each other. He ends with praise to God.

تصديق
أمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tasdiq without aman is not true faith but kufr.
Correction: The author shows this is logically impossible, as it confuses two opposite categories.

D) Making life death and death life.

Key Takeaway
Equating tasdiq without aman to kufr is a logical absurdity akin to calling opposites identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it take to see clearly and ask Allah to guide you to the truth?

The author ends with 'wa Allah al-muwaffiq'—acknowledging that only God grants success in recognizing truth. Do you truly rely on Allah to show you the reality of things, or do you trust your own reasoning to distinguish right from wrong? ?
The analogy of stillness and movement shows that calling a thing its opposite is impossible. Is there an area of your faith where you are trying to combine two opposites—like claiming to believe in Allah while living as if He does not see you? What 'stillness' have you called 'movement' in your own actions? Repent and seek His guidance to align your heart and deeds.

The Contradiction of the Committer of Major Sin
First internal contradiction: sahib al-kabira has tasdiq with aman but is not a believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the author exposes a contradiction in the Mu'tazila position by pointing out that the committer of a major sin fulfills their conditions for tasdiq bringing aman, yet is not considered a believer.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila contradict themselves: the sinner has tasdiq with aman but is not a believer.

After the initial reductio, the author attacks the Mu'tazila on their own terms. He presents the case of the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira), who according to Mu'tazila principles has tasdiq that does bring him into aman, yet they still deny him the status of believer. This reveals an internal inconsistency in their doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author says 'this statement is also false according to the principles of the Mu'tazila'.
He is using their own principles against them.
2
Identify the example
The example is the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira). According to Mu'tazila, his tasdiq does bring aman because it meets the definition and condition.
3
Spot the contradiction
Despite having tasdiq with aman, the Mu'tazila do not call this person a believer. This contradicts their claim that tasdiq without aman is kufr, because here tasdiq with aman is still not considered faith.
Thus, their position is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصديقه إيمانا لأنه لم يدخل نفسه في الأمان فلماذا جعلت تصديقه الثابت في الحقيقة تكذيبا؟ وهل هذا إلا كجعل السكون حركة، والسواد بياضا؟ وكل ذلك مستحيل متناقض والله الموفق. وهذا الكلام على أصول المعتزلة باطل أيضا، لأن صاحب الكبيرة وجد منه التصديق على وجه أدخل نفسه في الأمان، وتحقق بحده وشرطه وهو مع هذا ليس بمؤمن ومن توجهت عليه شبهة لم يتمكن من دفعها بعد وهو في طلب وجه الدفع، فلا أمان له من انعدام الظفر بما يتمكن به من دفعها وما لم يتمكن من ذلك لا يظهر أن ما ذهب إليه من العقيدة صحيح إذ الحق ما غلبت حُجَجه وأظهر أسباب التمويه في غيره، وإذا لم يظهر ذلك لم يعرف أنه لم يكن مكذوبا فيما أخبر بصحة هذا إذا كان في طلب دفعها ولم يضطرب في اعتقاده بقي مؤمنا، دل ومع أن هذا الكلام فاسد ولا استمرار لمذهبهم بل هي مذاهب متناقضة، ينقض البعض البعض، والحمد لله الذي هدانا لدينه القويم وصراطه المستقيم.
His tasdiq (assent) is faith because he did not enter himself into security (aman). So why do you make his tasdiq, which is established in reality, tantamount to disbelief? Is this not like making stillness movement, and blackness whiteness? All of that is impossible and contradictory, and God is the Grantor of success. This statement is also false according to the principles of the Mu'tazila, because the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has tasdiq from him in a manner that entered him into security, and is realized by its definition and condition, yet he is not a believer according to them. Also, one who is confronted by an ambiguity (shubha) which he cannot yet repel, while he is seeking a means of repelling it, has no security from failure to attain that by which he can repel it. And if he cannot do that, it does not become apparent that what he holds of belief is correct, for the truth is that whose evidences overcome and whose [opponent's] means of falsification become manifest. If that does not become apparent, it is not known that he was not lying regarding what he affirmed of the soundness of this [belief], while he is seeking to repel it and is not perturbed in his conviction, he remains a believer. [The text says: 'dalla wa ma'a anna hadha al-kalam fasid'] And this statement is corrupt and there is no consistency to their school; rather they are contradictory schools, some contradicting others. Praise be to God who guided us to His upright religion and straight path.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazila argument about the relationship between tasdiq (assent) and aman (security) in defining faith. The author first presents the Mu'tazila claim: if someone's tasdiq does not bring them into a state of aman, then it is not true faith but rather kufr. The author rejects this as impossible, comparing it to turning stillness into movement, a logical contradiction. He then argues that even on Mu'tazila principles this claim is false. He gives two examples: first, the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has tasdiq that does bring him into aman according to their own definition, yet they do not call him a believer—a contradiction. Second, a person faced with an ambiguity (shubha) that he cannot yet repel, while sincerely seeking to refute it, remains a believer because his inability does not negate his conviction. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila position is internally inconsistent and their different opinions contradict each other. He ends with praise to God.

Key Terms
كبيرة
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show the Mu'tazila contradict their own definition?
A) The committer of a major sin has tasdiq that brings aman yet is not considered a believer.
B) A Muslim who never commits sins always has aman.
C) The Mu'tazila require public declaration of faith.
D) The author shows that only prophets can have tasdiq with aman.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim collapses because their own example of the major sinner contradicts their definition of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself holding a belief that contradicts your own principles? How does the Mu'tazila's inconsistency challenge you to examine your own understanding of faith—do you sometimes define faith in a way that excludes actions, yet still consider yourself a believer? What would it take for you to align your definition with your actual beliefs?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever doubt your own faith? The Mu'tazila made a logical error by separating tasdiq from belief. How does this expose the danger of defining faith by human logic rather than revelation? In what areas of your life are you tempted to rely on your own reasoning over divine guidance, and what step can you take today to submit more fully?

The Case of the Ambiguity-Seeker
Second internal contradiction: the shubha-seeker lacks aman but remains a believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a person confronted with an unresolved ambiguity (shubha) but seeking to repel it remains a believer according to the author.

Core Idea
A believer facing an unresolved shubha remains a believer if he is seeking the truth.

The author continues his refutation by addressing another scenario: a person who encounters a theological ambiguity (shubha) that he cannot yet resolve. The Mu'tazila might argue that such a person lacks aman and therefore is not a believer. The author argues that as long as the person is sincerely seeking to repel the shubha and has not abandoned his conviction, he remains a believer. This further undermines the Mu'tazila equation of aman with faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. This is a longer, more involved argument. Pay attention to the condition and conclusion.
2
Understand the scenario
A person faces a shubha (ambiguity) that he cannot yet repel. He is actively seeking means to repel it. According to the Mu'tazila, he has no aman.
3
Follow the logic
The author argues that inability to repel does not show that his belief is false. Truth is determined by overwhelming evidence, not by the temporary inability to refute a doubt.
4
Identify the conclusion
As long as his heart is not perturbed and he is seeking the truth, he remains a believer. Thus, aman is not a condition for faith.
5
See the final critique
The author ends by declaring the Mu'tazila positions mutually contradictory and praises God for guidance.

Tasdiq Without Knowledge: Sufficiency for Faith
Defends sufficiency of tasdiq without knowledge against Mu'tazila objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why tasdiq alone suffices to exit disbelief and establish the rulings of Islam, even if not preceded by knowledge, according to the Maturidi response to the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
Tasdiq itself is the goal; knowledge is only a means, and the means drop when the end is achieved.

In this passage, the author addresses the Mu'tazila contention that valid tasdiq must be based on knowledge. He argues that even if knowledge is lacking, the tasdiq itself removes one from kufr and entitles one to the Islamic rulings. The author does not see the name 'believer' as crucial; what matters are the legal effects. He then presents a parallel argument: just as the Mu'tazila accept that one in a state of seeking knowledge is a believer, so too can tasdiq itself be sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening sentence: the Mu'tazila require that tasdiq be based on knowledge. Understand their position.
The author states their claim concisely.
2
Examine the author's response
Notice that the author concedes the point for argument but shifts the focus to the rulings (ahkam) rather than the name. He emphasizes that the goal is to establish Islamic rulings, not to argue about nomenclature.
Key phrase: 'We aim for nothing but this.'
3
Understand the analogy
The author uses two analogies: (1) inference and knowledge are means to tasdiq, and once the objective is reached, the means are unnecessary. (2) The Mu'tazila themselves consider a seeker who has not yet attained knowledge as a believer due to his state. The same principle applies here.
Follow the reasoning: means vs. end, and the countervailing condition (mu'arada).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن التصديق ينبغي أن يكون عن علم، قلنا: ومع هذا عند الأشعري بهذا التصديق، يخرج عن الكفر ويستحق الثواب الموعود للمؤمن، ونحن لا نروم إلا هذا، إذ غرضنا من هذا إثبات أحكام الإسلام، لا إطلاق العبارة إذ لا ينفع بإطلاق التسمية مع انعدام الأحكام، ولا ضرر بزوال التسمية مع بقاء أحكام الإسلام، ثم نقول له: كل عذر لك في إثبات الأحكام فهو عذرنا في إطلاق الاسم، يحققه أن الاستدلال وتحصيل العلم ليتوصل بهما إلى التصديق الذي هو المقصد المأمور به، فإذ وصل إلى المقصود به وأتى بما أمر على وجهه كان معتبرا، ولا عبرة لانعدام ما ثبت ذريعة له عند حصول المقصود بدونه وكذا بهذا عند المعتزلة يخرج عن الكفر، يحققه أن حالة طلب ما يتمكن به من دفع الشبهة كان مؤمنا وإن انعدم العلم لثبوت المعارضة للحال لما ذكرنا وكذا هذا، وكل ما يعتمدون عليه هناك فهو معتمدنا هاهنا.
And what they said: 'Tasdiq should be based on knowledge.' We say: Even so, according to al-Ash'ari, by this tasdiq one exits from kufr and deserves the promised reward for the believer. We aim for nothing but this, for our purpose in this is to establish the rulings of Islam, not to pronounce the expression [of faith], since the pronouncement of the name is of no benefit when the rulings are absent, and there is no harm in the removal of the name while the rulings of Islam remain. Then we say to him: Every excuse you have in establishing the rulings is our excuse in applying the name. This is substantiated by the fact that inference (istidlal) and acquisition of knowledge are employed to reach the tasdiq which is the commanded objective. So when one attains the objective by it and performs what is commanded in its proper manner, it is considered valid, and no attention is paid to the absence of what was established as a means to it when the objective is achieved without it. Similarly, by this, according to the Mu'tazila, one exits from kufr. This is substantiated by the fact that the one in a state of seeking what enables him to repel the ambiguity is a believer even if knowledge is absent, due to the existence of a countervailing condition (al-mu'arada li'l-hal) as we mentioned. And this is similar. And everything they rely on there is our reliance here.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a response to the Mu'tazila objection that tasdiq must be based on knowledge to be valid. The author first concedes the point for argument's sake, saying that even if that is the case, according to al-Ash'ari, such tasdiq still removes one from kufr and entitles one to reward. The author states that their goal is simply to establish the legal rulings of Islam (ahkam al-Islam), not to argue about the name. The name is secondary; what matters are the rulings. Then the author argues that whatever excuse the Mu'tazila use to establish the rulings (e.g., that tasdiq with knowledge suffices) can be used by us to apply the name. He substantiates: inference (istidlal) and acquiring knowledge are means to tasdiq, but when the objective is reached, the means are no longer required. Similarly, in the case of one seeking to repel a shubha—even without attained knowledge, he is a believer because his state (hal) of seeking makes him so. Thus, the same principle applies here: tasdiq itself, even without prior knowledge, suffices for the rulings.

Key Terms
تصديق
علم
أشعري
أحكام الإسلام
استدلال
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is a necessary condition for the legal effects of faith.
Correction: The author shows that tasdiq (affirmation) itself suffices to establish the rulings; knowledge is only a means that can be absent without harm.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does tasdiq suffice even without prior knowledge?
A) Because inference and acquisition of knowledge are not commanded.
B) Because the objective is tasdiq, and once attained, the means are not necessary.
C) Because the Mu'tazila also allow it.
D) Because al-Ash'ari made it a condition.

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq itself, not necessarily preceded by knowledge, is sufficient to leave kufr and establish Islamic rulings; the means are subordinate to the objective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If tomorrow you lost all ability to argue for your faith rationally, would your tasdiq still be enough to keep you a believer in Allah's sight? Examine your heart: do you rely more on your knowledge or on your affirmation?
?
The author says there is no harm in losing the name 'believer' as long as the rulings remain. Are you more attached to being called a believer in public than to actually having the reality of iman in your life? What would change if people no longer considered you a believer?
?
The Mu'tazila were inconsistent: they accepted tasdiq without knowledge in one case but not another. Are you consistent in how you judge others' faith? Do you hold yourself to a different standard? Commit today to applying the same mercy and logic to others that you apply to yourself.
