The Self-Defeating Claim about Shubha and Kufr
Author refutes Mu'tazila claim about shubha causing kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim that inability to repel a shubha causes kufr is self-defeating.

Core Idea
If shubha forces kufr, then every leader would be a kafir.

The Mu'tazila argued that being unable to repel a doubt (shubha) makes one a disbeliever, even if one seeks to repel it. The author responds with a powerful reductio ad absurdum: if this were true, then all people, especially their own leaders and imams, would have been guilty of kufr at some point. This lesson walks through that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
First, understand the position being refuted: that being unable to repel a shubha at the moment causes kufr, even if one is trying to repel it.
This is a key belief of the Mu'tazila on the nature of faith.
2
Identify the reductio
Notice how the author does not argue from scripture but from the logical consequences of their own claim. He shows that it would force them to admit kufr against themselves, their leaders, and all imams.
The argument proceeds in three stages: themselves, leaders, imams.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that since every leader has faced an irresolvable shubha and resorted to something impossible (muhal), by their own standard they would be considered kafir even by their own followers.
This makes the claim untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كابروا وقالوا: لما اعترضت الشبهة ولم يتمكن للحال من دفعها يكفر وإن كان في طلب دفعها، فقيل لهم: قد أقررتم على أنفسكم أولا، ثم على كل رئيس من رؤسائكم ثانيا، ثم على كل إمام كان في الأمة ثالثا، بالكفر وبطلان الأعمال مرارا إذ لا يخلو أحد من ذلك في عمره في الأحايين، ثم نقول لهم: ما من رئيس من رؤسائكم إلا وقد اعترضت له شبهة لم يتمكن من دفعها، وارتكب لأجلها محالا أكفره به جميع أهل نحلته فضلا عمن خالفهم فيكونون كلهم كفارا.
If they persist and say: When an ambiguity (shubha) confronts one and he is unable at that moment to repel it, he becomes a disbeliever even if he is seeking to repel it. Then it is said to them: You have admitted against yourselves first, then against every leader (ra'is) of your leaders second, then against every imam in the community third, disbelief and invalidity of deeds repeatedly, since no one is free from that in his lifetime at some times. Then we say to them: There is no leader among your leaders except that he was confronted by an ambiguity which he could not repel, and he committed for its sake something impossible (muhal), which all the people of his group considered him a disbeliever for, let alone those who differed with them; thus they would all be disbelievers.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The Mu'tazila make a further claim: if a person is confronted by a shubha and cannot repel it at that moment, he becomes a kafir (disbeliever) even if he is seeking to repel it. The author responds with a reductio ad absurdum. First, he says that this logic would force them to admit that every person, including themselves and their leaders, has been in such a state at some point, thus they would all be guilty of kufr at times. Then he specifies: every leader among them has faced a shubha he could not repel, and often resorted to an impossibility (muhal) in trying to resolve it, which even their own followers would consider kufr. Consequently, the Mu'tazila would have to regard themselves and their entire community as disbelievers. This shows the self-defeating nature of their claim.

Key Terms
شبهة
كفر
رئيس
إمام
محال
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who is unable to repel a doubt (shubha) at a given moment becomes a disbeliever, even if he is trying to repel it.
Correction: This belief leads to absurd consequences, including that all people, especially their own leaders, would be disbelievers. Therefore, the claim is false.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the Mu'tazila claim about shubha were true?
A) Only common people would be disbelievers.
B) Their own leaders would be guilty of kufr.
C) Only those who do not seek to repel the shubha would be kafir.
D) The community would be split into believers and disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim that shubha causes kufr is self-refuting because it would imply their own leaders are disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself uncertain about a theological matter, do you immediately assume your faith is in jeopardy? How does the author’s argument—that even the greatest leaders faced irresolvable doubts without losing their Islam—change your panic into patience? Will you now give yourself the same grace you extend to others?
?
Think of a time you declared someone else's Islam weak or invalid because they expressed doubt. Based on this lesson, if you applied the same standard to yourself, where would you stand? Are you willing to surrender your right to judge others and instead trust that Allah knows the heart's struggle?
?
The Mu'tazila claim seems strict but logically self-defeating. What claim or standard do you hold for others that, if honestly applied to your own life, would expose you as a hypocrite? Will you ask Allah tonight to show you one such area and give you the courage to abandon it?

The Independent Existence of Knowledge and Tasdiq
Author proves knowledge and tasdiq can exist independently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge (ilm) and assent (tasdiq) are distinct and can exist independently, using the examples of faith in the unseen and the obstinate recognition of truth.

Core Idea
Knowledge and assent are separate: one can exist without the other.

In this passage, the author addresses those who conflate knowledge and tasdiq by presenting two clear examples. He shows that believers affirm the existence of angels, Books, and Messengers without knowing their individual identities, while the obstinate (mu'anidun) recognize the truth but refuse to assent. This demonstrates the permissibility of their separation from both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's main argument and examples.
2
Identify the two proofs
The author provides two scenarios: one where tasdiq exists without knowledge, and one where knowledge exists without tasdiq. Note each example and reflect on how they support the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic citation
The author quotes Qur'an 6:20 about the People of the Book. Consider how this verse supports the claim that knowledge can exist without tasdiq.
4
Consider the theological implications
Think about why this distinction matters for debates on faith and disbelief, especially regarding the Mu'tazila and Jahm ibn Safwan.
This connects to broader sectarian discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: ليس من ضرورة انعدام العلم، انعدام التصديق، فإنا آمنا بالملائكة كلهم وكذا بالكتب والرسل ولا نعرفهم بأبدانهم، والمعاندون يعرفون ولا يصدقون على ما قال الله في علماء أهل الكتاب: يعْرِفُونَهُ كَمَا يَعْرِفُونَ أَبْنَاءَهُم فدل ذلك على جواز انفكاك التصديق عن العلم من الجانبين جميعا.
Then we say to them: It is not a necessary consequence of the absence of knowledge that tasdiq is absent. For we believe in all the angels, and likewise in the Books and Messengers, yet we do not know them by their bodies. And the obstinate (mu'anidun) know [the Prophet] but do not assent, as God said concerning the scholars of the People of the Book: 'They recognize him as they recognize their sons' (Qur'an 6:20). So that proves the permissibility of the separation of tasdiq from knowledge from both sides.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a proof that knowledge and tasdiq (assent) are distinct and can exist separately. The author argues from two directions. First, believers affirm (tasdiq) the existence of angels, Books, and Messengers without having detailed knowledge of their individual identities or bodies. Thus tasdiq exists without knowledge. Second, the obstinate (mu'anidun), like the Jewish scholars, recognized the Prophet Muhammad with knowledge (they knew him from their scriptures), yet they did not assent (does not have tasdiq). The author cites Quran 6:20 to support this: 'They recognize him as they recognize their sons.' So knowledge exists without tasdiq. Therefore, tasdiq and knowledge are separable. This refutes the Mu'tazila claim that tasdiq must be accompanied by knowledge at all times, and also rejects Jahm ibn Safwan's equation of iman with pure knowledge (ma'rifa).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that belief (tasdiq) always requires detailed knowledge, or that knowledge always leads to belief.
Correction: The author shows that tasdiq can exist without knowledge (as in faith in the unseen), and knowledge can exist without tasdiq (as in the case of the obstinate).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and tasdiq are two distinct mental acts that can occur independently, as demonstrated by the examples of faith in the unseen and the obstinate recognition of truth.

?

Affirmation Can Exist Without Knowledge
The author proves that knowledge and affirmation can be separate, using the example of angels and the People of the Book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how affirmation (tasdiq) can exist without detailed knowledge, and how knowledge can exist without affirmation, using examples from Islamic belief and the People of the Book.

Core Idea
Affirmation and knowledge can be separate; one does not require the other.

This lesson examines a key argument from Imam al-Nasafi about the relationship between knowledge and affirmation. The Mu'tazila claimed that faith requires detailed knowledge, but the author shows that this is not the case. Through two examples, he demonstrates that affirmation can exist without knowledge, and knowledge can exist without affirmation. This is crucial for understanding the nature of faith.

Guided Path

Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation to capture the argument's flow.

3

This connects to the broader debate about the definition of faith.

Commentary
This passage provides a separate proof. The author argues that lack of knowledge does not necessarily imply lack of affirmation (tasdiq). He gives two examples: Muslims affirm the existence of angels, books, and messengers without having direct knowledge of their physical forms. Conversely, the People of the Book recognized the truth about the Prophet Muhammad (as described in their scriptures) but did not affirm it. This shows that affirmation can exist without detailed knowledge, and knowledge can exist without affirmation. Thus, the Mu'tazila's insistence on knowledge as a condition for faith is refuted.

The Muqallid as a Scholar: Implicit Proof
Argument that muqallid is a scholar via implicit proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the muqallid (blind follower) can be considered a scholar because his belief is based on an implicit proof, even without explicit demonstrative reasoning.

Core Idea
Even blind faith rests on some proof, making the follower a scholar.

This lesson explores a fascinating defense of the muqallid's status. Some scholars argued that the muqallid is not merely a blind follower but actually a scholar because his acceptance of faith is grounded in a proof—either the perceived trustworthiness of the person who invited him or the evident merits of the religion itself. This implicit proof, combined with certainty, elevates him to the rank of a scholar. Let's examine this argument as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument. Note that the text is incomplete at the end.
The passage ends abruptly, so focus on the complete portion.
2
Identify the two bases for implicit proof
The text mentions two ways the muqallid arrives at proof: from the trustworthiness of the caller (who must not be a liar) or from observing the merits of the religion itself. Notice how each serves as a foundation for belief.
These are the key components that make the muqallid a scholar.
3
Understand why this makes him a scholar
Even though each individual indication is probabilistic, when the muqallid builds his belief on it without doubting its falsehood, and the belief matches reality, he is considered to have knowledge. Reflect on this reasoning.
4
Consider the incomplete ending
The text cuts off with a phrase that likely applies this ruling to a specific scenario. Think about how the argument might be extended or qualified.
This part is unclear due to the incomplete text.

And some of them said: He is a scholar, because even if he is not a scholar through a proof, there is no doubt that a person does not leave his religion, which is dearer to him than his life, wealth, family, and children, except by a kind of proof that appears to him, either from the aspect that the one who called him to the religion does not lie, or from the aspect that he sees the merits of the religion, so he builds his belief upon it. And it is in general a proof, even if any single (indication) is probabilistic. But when he builds his belief upon it and does not conceive the possibility of it being false, and the believed thing is as he believed, then he is considered a scholar. Then he applied this issue concerning one who was... for what came.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative view. Some scholars argue that the muqallid is actually a scholar (عالم) because his belief is based on a proof, even if it is not an explicit demonstrative proof. The proof could be the perceived trustworthiness of the person who invited him to Islam, or the apparent merits of the religion itself. Although each individual indication may be probabilistic, when combined with the muqallid's certainty and lack of doubt about its falsehood, it becomes a sufficient basis for knowledge. Therefore, he is considered knowledgeable. The text then cuts off, mentioning that this issue was applied to someone who ... (incomplete).

The Muqallid's Implicit Proof Makes Him a Scholar
Presents the argument that the muqallid is a scholar and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars consider a muqallid to be a scholar based on the implicit proof he holds.

Core Idea
Implicit trust in a truthful caller is itself a form of proof.

This lesson examines a second opinion on the muqallid: that he is actually a scholar because he does not abandon his religion without some reason, even if not formally articulated. We'll see how this reasoning applies to a person raised in isolation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: general principle then example.
2
Identify the key argument
The passage argues that a person does not leave his religion except with some kind of proof. Find that statement and see how it defines 'proof' broadly.
3
Apply to the isolated individual
The author gives an example of someone raised in isolation who accepts Islam after meeting a Muslim. Consider whether his acceptance counts as proof-based.
This is the point of disagreement among scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : هو عالم لأنه وإن لم يكن عالما بدليل لكن لا شك أن الإنسان لا يترك دينه الذي هو أعز عنده من نفسه وماله وأهله وولده إلا بضرب دليل يبدو له إما من جهة هذا الذي دعاه إلى دين أنه لا يكذب أو من جهة الدين يرى من محاسنه فيبني اعتقاده عليه وهو يصلح في الجملة دليلا، وإن كان الواحد محتملا ولكنه لما بنى اعتقاده عليه ولم يخطر بباله جواز كونه كاذبا وكان المعتقد كما اعتقد، فأنزل عالما، ثم نزل هذه المسألة في حق من كان ينشأ في قطر من الأقطار، أو شاهق جبل من الجبال، لم تبلغه الدعوى ولا علم بثبوت هذه الملة، فشاهده مسلم فدعاه إلى الدين، وبين له ما يجب اعتقاده، وأُخْبِرَ رسولا لنا بلغ هذا الدين عن الله ودعانا إليه وقد ظهرت على يده المعجزات الناقضات للعادة، فصدقه هذا الإنسان في جميع ذلك واعتقد الدين من غير سابقة تأمل وتفكر هذا هو الذي اختلفوا فيه، فأما أهل دار الإسلام عوامهم وخواصهم نسوانهم وصبيانهم، العاقلون أهل الأمصار والرساتيق والقرى، وسكان الصحاري والبراري فكلهم مؤمنون مسلمون عارفون الله تعالى ووحدانيته وغير ذلك لن يخلو أحد منهم عن ضرب الاستدلال، وإن كان لا يهتدي إلى العبارة عن دليله، ولا يقدر على دفع الشبه المعترضة حتى إن واحدا منهم متى عاين هؤلاء من الأهوال كرعد هائل وهبوب ريح عاصفة أو ظلمة راكدة يسبح للحال، ويصف الله تعالى بكمال القدرة ونفاذ المشيئة وتمام العلم والحكمة علما منه أن لا تعلق لهذه الأفزاع إلا بقدرته التامة ومشيئته النفاذة، وهو الذي خلق السماوات بغير عمد ممدودة وأطناب مشدودة، وجعل فيها الأفلاك الدائرة، والنجوم السائرة، وخلق الأرض وجعل فيها الجبال الواسعة، وشق فيها الأنهار الجارية، على هذا أهل جميع الأسواق والنواحي والقرى والرساتيق والنسوان والعقلاء من الصبيان، فلم يكن في هؤلاء بين مشايخنا خلاف ولا بيننا وبين الأشعري، وإنما الخلاف فيهم بيننا وبين المعتزلة على ما مر، والحمد لله على التوفيق والهداية.
And among them is one who said: He is a scholar, because even if he is not a scholar with a proof, there is no doubt that a man does not abandon his religion—which is dearer to him than himself, his wealth, his family, and his child—except by a kind of proof that appears to him, either from the side of the one who called him to the religion, that he does not lie, or from the side of the religion, he sees its excellences and builds his belief upon it, and that is in general suitable as a proof, even if individually it is probable. But when he built his belief upon it and did not conceive the possibility of his being a liar, and the object of belief was as he believed, then he is considered a scholar. Then he applied this issue to one who grows up in a region of regions, or on a high mountain of mountains, to whom the call has not reached nor does he know of the establishment of this creed. Then a Muslim saw him, called him to the religion, and explained to him what he must believe, and he was informed that a messenger of ours conveyed this religion from God and called us to it, and miracles breaking custom appeared at his hand. So this human being believed him in all that and believed the religion without prior reflection and thought. This is that which they differed over. As for the people of the Abode of Islam, their commoners and their elite, their women and their children, the rational ones among the people of the cities, the rural districts, and the villages, and the inhabitants of deserts and wildernesses—all of them are believers, Muslims, knowers of God Most High and His Oneness and other matters. Not one of them is free from a kind of inference, even if he cannot articulate his proof nor is able to repel the objections that arise. So much so that if one of them witnesses among these horrors—a tremendous thunder, a violent wind, or a dense darkness—he immediately glorifies God and describes God Most High with perfect power, effective will, complete knowledge and wisdom, knowing that these terrors are connected only to His perfect power and effective will. And He is the One who created the heavens without extended supports or tightened ropes, and placed in them the rotating spheres and the traveling stars, and created the earth and placed in it the vast mountains and split in it the flowing rivers. Upon this are all the people of the markets, regions, villages, rural districts, women, and the rational ones among children. So there was no disagreement among our masters concerning these people, nor between us and al-Ash'ari. Rather, the disagreement concerning them is between us and the Mu'tazila, as has passed. And praise be to God for success and guidance.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a second opinion on the muqallid: some scholars say he is actually a scholar (''alim'). The argument is that even without an explicit formal proof, a person does not abandon his religion—which he holds dearer than life—without some kind of reason. This reason may be trust in the truthfulness of the caller or appreciation of the religion's virtues. Although such reasoning may be merely probable, it still provides a basis for belief, and if the person does not doubt it, he is considered a scholar. The author then applies this to a specific case: a person raised in isolation who meets a Muslim, learns about Islam, and accepts it after hearing about the Prophet's miracles. This case is the one scholars differed over regarding whether such a person is a scholar. The author then asserts a broad consensus: all Muslims within the Islamic realm—commoners, elites, women, children, urban and rural—are believers and knowers of God, because none of them is entirely devoid of some form of inference (istidlal), even if they cannot articulate it. As evidence, the author describes how even a simple person, upon witnessing a natural phenomenon like thunder, automatically attributes it to God's power and wisdom, which is an implicit inference. The passage concludes that there is no disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris on this matter; the only disagreement is with the Mu'tazila, who presumably held that only those with explicit demonstrative knowledge are true believers.

Key Terms
المقلد
العالم
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only scholars with formal demonstrative proofs have true faith and can be considered 'alim.
Correction: This passage shows that implicit trust in a truthful caller or the religion's virtues, when held without doubt, constitutes a form of proof that qualifies the person as a scholar.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The muqallid can be considered a scholar because he holds an implicit proof, even if not articulated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Universal Istidlal: All Muslims Have Implicit Inference
Shows that all Muslims have istidlal and there is consensus with Ash'aris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all Muslims, even commoners, possess a form of rational inference (istidlal) in their faith.

Even the simplest person infers God's power from natural phenomena.

After discussing the muqallid, the author broadens the scope to show that all Muslims within the Islamic realm are true believers because they all engage in some form of inference, whether articulate or not. The example of someone witnessing thunder demonstrates this innate reasoning.

ومنهم من قال : هو عالم لأنه وإن لم يكن عالما بدليل لكن لا شك أن الإنسان لا يترك دينه الذي هو أعز عنده من نفسه وماله وأهله وولده إلا بضرب دليل يبدو له إما من جهة هذا الذي دعاه إلى دين أنه لا يكذب أو من جهة الدين يرى من محاسنه فيبني اعتقاده عليه وهو يصلح في الجملة دليلا، وإن كان الواحد محتملا ولكنه لما بنى اعتقاده عليه ولم يخطر بباله جواز كونه كاذبا وكان المعتقد كما اعتقد، فأنزل عالما، ثم نزل هذه المسألة في حق من كان ينشأ في قطر من الأقطار، أو شاهق جبل من الجبال، لم تبلغه الدعوى ولا علم بثبوت هذه الملة، فشاهده مسلم فدعاه إلى الدين، وبين له ما يجب اعتقاده، وأُخْبِرَ رسولا لنا بلغ هذا الدين عن الله ودعانا إليه وقد ظهرت على يده المعجزات الناقضات للعادة، فصدقه هذا الإنسان في جميع ذلك واعتقد الدين من غير سابقة تأمل وتفكر هذا هو الذي اختلفوا فيه، فأما أهل دار الإسلام عوامهم وخواصهم نسوانهم وصبيانهم، العاقلون أهل الأمصار والرساتيق والقرى، وسكان الصحاري والبراري فكلهم مؤمنون مسلمون عارفون الله تعالى ووحدانيته وغير ذلك لن يخلو أحد منهم عن ضرب الاستدلال، وإن كان لا يهتدي إلى العبارة عن دليله، ولا يقدر على دفع الشبه المعترضة حتى إن واحدا منهم متى عاين هؤلاء من الأهوال كرعد هائل وهبوب ريح عاصفة أو ظلمة راكدة يسبح للحال، ويصف الله تعالى بكمال القدرة ونفاذ المشيئة وتمام العلم والحكمة علما منه أن لا تعلق لهذه الأفزاع إلا بقدرته التامة ومشيئته النفاذة، وهو الذي خلق السماوات بغير عمد ممدودة وأطناب مشدودة، وجعل فيها الأفلاك الدائرة، والنجوم السائرة، وخلق الأرض وجعل فيها الجبال الواسعة، وشق فيها الأنهار الجارية، على هذا أهل جميع الأسواق والنواحي والقرى والرساتيق والنسوان والعقلاء من الصبيان، فلم يكن في هؤلاء بين مشايخنا خلاف ولا بيننا وبين الأشعري، وإنما الخلاف فيهم بيننا وبين المعتزلة على ما مر، والحمد لله على التوفيق والهداية.
And among them is one who said: He is a scholar, because even if he is not a scholar with a proof, there is no doubt that a man does not abandon his religion—which is dearer to him than himself, his wealth, his family, and his child—except by a kind of proof that appears to him, either from the side of the one who called him to the religion, that he does not lie, or from the side of the religion, he sees its excellences and builds his belief upon it, and that is in general suitable as a proof, even if individually it is probable. But when he built his belief upon it and did not conceive the possibility of his being a liar, and the object of belief was as he believed, then he is considered a scholar. Then he applied this issue to one who grows up in a region of regions, or on a high mountain of mountains, to whom the call has not reached nor does he know of the establishment of this creed. Then a Muslim saw him, called him to the religion, and explained to him what he must believe, and he was informed that a messenger of ours conveyed this religion from God and called us to it, and miracles breaking custom appeared at his hand. So this human being believed him in all that and believed the religion without prior reflection and thought. This is that which they differed over. As for the people of the Abode of Islam, their commoners and their elite, their women and their children, the rational ones among the people of the cities, the rural districts, and the villages, and the inhabitants of deserts and wildernesses—all of them are believers, Muslims, knowers of God Most High and His Oneness and other matters. Not one of them is free from a kind of inference, even if he cannot articulate his proof nor is able to repel the objections that arise. So much so that if one of them witnesses among these horrors—a tremendous thunder, a violent wind, or a dense darkness—he immediately glorifies God and describes God Most High with perfect power, effective will, complete knowledge and wisdom, knowing that these terrors are connected only to His perfect power and effective will. And He is the One who created the heavens without extended supports or tightened ropes, and placed in them the rotating spheres and the traveling stars, and created the earth and placed in it the vast mountains and split in it the flowing rivers. Upon this are all the people of the markets, regions, villages, rural districts, women, and the rational ones among children. So there was no disagreement among our masters concerning these people, nor between us and al-Ash'ari. Rather, the disagreement concerning them is between us and the Mu'tazila, as has passed. And praise be to God for success and guidance.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a second opinion on the muqallid: some scholars say he is actually a scholar (''alim'). The argument is that even without an explicit formal proof, a person does not abandon his religion—which he holds dearer than life—without some kind of reason. This reason may be trust in the truthfulness of the caller or appreciation of the religion's virtues. Although such reasoning may be merely probable, it still provides a basis for belief, and if the person does not doubt it, he is considered a scholar. The author then applies this to a specific case: a person raised in isolation who meets a Muslim, learns about Islam, and accepts it after hearing about the Prophet's miracles. This case is the one scholars differed over regarding whether such a person is a scholar. The author then asserts a broad consensus: all Muslims within the Islamic realm—commoners, elites, women, children, urban and rural—are believers and knowers of God, because none of them is entirely devoid of some form of inference (istidlal), even if they cannot articulate it. As evidence, the author describes how even a simple person, upon witnessing a natural phenomenon like thunder, automatically attributes it to God's power and wisdom, which is an implicit inference. The passage concludes that there is no disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris on this matter; the only disagreement is with the Mu'tazila, who presumably held that only those with explicit demonstrative knowledge are true believers.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
المعجزات الناقضات للعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only scholars who can articulate formal proofs are true believers and have knowledge of God.
Correction: This passage shows that all Muslims, including commoners and children, engage in implicit inference from natural phenomena, which suffices for true belief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who among the following is included in the universal claim that all Muslims have istidlal?
A) Only the scholars and elites.
B) Only the men who live in cities.
C) All Muslims: commoners, elite, women, children, urban and rural.
D) Only those who can articulate their proofs.

Key Takeaway
All Muslims, regardless of station, have a basic rational inference embedded in their faith, confirming the universality of istidlal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden terrifying event like a thunderstorm or earthquake, what is your immediate inner reaction? Does your heart turn to God spontaneously, or do you only think of natural causes? This lesson says even a simple person instinctively glorifies God—examine whether your own reaction aligns with that innate fitrah.
?
Are you ever ashamed that you cannot defend your faith with sophisticated philosophical arguments like the scholars? This lesson teaches that even if you cannot articulate proof, you already have implicit inference. How does this reality change the way you view your own faith and that of others who may be less educated?
?
Think of a Muslim you know who seems simple or uneducated but has deep devotion. Based on this lesson, how do you think God views their faith? Are you tempted to look down on them? Let this passage correct your heart and fill you with respect for the universal gift of istidlal.

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

The Dilemma of Essence vs. Meaning in Faith and Disobedience
Nasafi refutes the denier of accidents by a dilemma: actions are either essence or meaning, forcing admission of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the denial of accidents by showing that moral and religious states like faith and disobedience must be accidents beyond the essence.

Core Idea
Faith and disobedience cannot be the essence itself — they must be accidents.

In this passage, Nasafi presents a powerful argument against those who deny the existence of accidents (a‛rāḍ). He uses the very human experiences of faith, obedience, anger, and praise to show that these states cannot be identical with the essence of a person. Instead, they must be additional meanings — that is, accidents. By forcing his opponent into a dilemma, he demonstrates that denying accidents leads to absurdity or self-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the repeated pattern: a question with two possible answers, each leading to a conclusion that the opponent cannot accept.
The opponent is called 'al-asamm' (the deaf) — a label for the denier of accidents.
2
Analyze the first dilemma (faith/disbelief)
Identify the two horns: either the reward/punishment attaches to the essence (absurd, because essence is the same in both states) or to a meaning beyond the essence (concedes accidents). Understand why the essence option is untenable.
Nasafi uses the fact that a single person can be both believer and disbeliever at different times.
3
Examine the second and third examples
The command to believe and the insult/praise examples follow the same logic. Notice how each forces the same admission: the state in question must be an accident.
The third example (insult/praise) adds the nuance that emotions like anger and pleasure are not identical to the essence.
4
Understand the conclusion
Nasafi states that denying accidents is 'denial of necessities and denial of observable facts.' Reflect on why this is a strong indictment.
5
Consider the incomplete question about 'muftari'
The passage ends with 'What is the definition of muftari? He must say: ...' Think about how this question would continue the same argument — the definition of a fabricator would involve a meaning (accident) beyond the essence.
This shows the argument is extendable to other terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا أسئلة من تأمل عرف جوابها، فأعرضنا عن ذكرها مخافة الإطالة، وهذا الإلزام ثابت في جميع الأعراض التي تتعاقب هي وأضدادها على محل واحد. ويقال للأصم: أليس أن من آمن كان مطيعا وينال الثواب، ومن كفر كان عاصيا ويستحق العقاب، وكذا عن كل طاعة ومعصية؟ أفيتعلق كل ذلك بوجود ذاته أم بوجود معنى وراء ذاته؟ فإن قال بذاته بان بهته وظهرت مكابرته، وإن قال بمعنى وراء ذاته فقد ترك مذهبه وانقاد للحق، ويقال له المأمور بالإيمان وغيره من الطاعات أمأمور به بتحصيل نفسه أم بتحصيل معنى غير نفسه؟ فإن قال بالأول ظهر عناده، وإن قال بالثاني فقد ترك مذهبهم، وكذا شتم غيره بسخطه ومدحه يرضيه والرضا والسخط لا يتعلقان بذات المادح والشاتم ولا رضاه وسخطه يرجعان إلى ذاته وعرف بهذا أن إنكار الأعراض من قبيل جحد الضروريات وإنكار المشاهدات، وكذا يقال: ما حد المفتري؟ فلابد من أن يقول:
And upon this [principle] there are questions; whoever reflects knows their answers, so we refrain from mentioning them for fear of prolixity. And this binding argument is established for all accidents that alternate with their opposites upon a single subject. And it is said to the deaf [denier]: Is it not that whoever believes is obedient and attains reward, and whoever disbelieves is disobedient and deserves punishment, and likewise for every act of obedience and disobedience? Does all that relate to the existence of his essence or to the existence of a meaning beyond his essence? If he says: To his essence, then his bewilderment becomes apparent and his obstinacy shows; and if he says: To a meaning beyond his essence, then he has abandoned his school and submitted to the truth. And it is said to him: The one commanded to believe and other acts of obedience — is he commanded to procure his own essence or to procure a meaning other than his essence? If he says the first, his obstinacy appears; and if he says the second, he has abandoned his school. Likewise, [if] he insults another out of anger and praises [him] to please him, while pleasure and anger do not relate to the essence of the praiser or the insulter, nor does his pleasure and anger revert to his essence. And it is known by this that the denial of accidents is a type of denial of necessities and denial of observable facts. And likewise it is said: What is the definition of 'muftari' (fabricator)? He must say: ...
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage extends the argument against those who deny the existence of accidents (a‘rad), here called 'al-asamm' (the deaf) as a label for such a denier. The author first notes that many questions follow from the established principle, which he omits for brevity, and that the binding argument applies to all accidents that alternate with their opposites on a single subject. The argument then proceeds: The denier is asked whether the obedient believer attaining reward and the disobedient disbeliever deserving punishment are due to the existence of his essence (dhat) or to a meaning (ma‘na) beyond his essence. If he says 'due to essence,' his position is clearly absurd because the essence is the same in both states. If he says 'due to a meaning,' he admits the existence of an accident (the meaning), thus abandoning his denial. Next, the author uses the command to believe and obey: is the person commanded to produce his own essence or to produce a meaning other than his essence? Again, the first is absurd, the second concedes accidents. Lastly, the example of insulting out of anger and praising to please: pleasure and anger do not pertain to the essence of the person, so they must be accidents. The passage concludes that denying accidents amounts to denying necessary truths and observable facts. The final question 'What is the definition of muftari?' is left incomplete, but it implies that the denier would have to define a fabricator (muftari) in a way that also forces admission of accidents.

ذات
معنى
عرض
مفتري
طاعة
معصية
إيمان
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only applies to religious actions like faith and disobedience.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly states that the binding argument is established for all accidents that alternate with their opposites, not just religious acts.

Checkpoint
What does the author force the denier of accidents (al-asamm) to admit regarding the relationship between faith/disbelief and the essence?
A) Faith and disbelief are identical with the essence
B) Faith and disbelief are due to a meaning (ma'na) beyond the essence
C) Faith and disbelief do not exist
D) Faith and disbelief are only names with no reality

Key Takeaway
Denying accidents forces one into logical contradictions, as moral and emotional states cannot be the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith and disobedience are not part of your essence but are accidents you acquire, then what are you actively doing today to ‘procure’ the meaning of obedience? Are you treating your actions as real, choice-driven accidents that define your fate, or do you live as if your essence is already fixed and nothing you do matters?
?
The passage forces you to admit that reward and punishment cannot attach to your essence alone. So, when you sin, do you truly believe that your essence remains untouched, or do you feel the weight of that accident changing your standing before Allah? If you deny accidents, you deny that your actions have any real effect—are you willing to live with that consequence of your own theology?
?
You insult someone out of anger and praise someone to please them. That anger and pleasure are not your essence, but they are real and you are accountable for them. Look at your last emotional outburst: did you treat it as a passing accident that doesn't define you, or did you recognize it as a real attribute for which you will be questioned? What would it mean to truly own your accidents?

Linking Creation to Eternity: Ash'ari Objections
Author defends his method, reports first two Ash'ari objections linking to Ibn Qals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari linked the Maturidi position on khalq and makhluq to the claim of the world's eternity, and why the author insists on quoting opponents verbatim.

Core Idea
Quoting opponents fully shows commitment to truth, not bias.

In this passage, the author begins by defending his method of reporting opponents' arguments verbatim. He then shows how a second Ash'ari writer tried to discredit the Maturidi view by associating it with the claim of the world's eternity (citing Ibn Qals). This sets the stage for the third Ash'ari's direct refutation, which we will examine in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's defense
Notice how the author explains why he quotes the Ash'ari exactly: to avoid accusations of misrepresentation and to show that he follows proof, not bias.
The author is a Maturidi defending his methodology.
2
Identify the first Ash'ari's action
The author reports that an Ash'ari spoke about this matter, and he narrates it in his words. Then a second Ash'ari takes it further.
3
Understand the second Ash'ari's strategy
This Ash'ari includes the Maturidi argument in his book and links it to Ibn Qals' claim that the world is eternal because God's generosity is eternal. This is a rhetorical move to discredit the Maturidi position by association.
Ibn Qals was known for arguing the eternity of the world from God's generosity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتكلم بهذا كله بعض الأشعرية حكيته بلفظه لئلا يظن بي التقصير في حكاية شبه الخصوم. والحكاية على وجه يسهل على الجواب، بل يعلم أن متبع الدليل منقاد للحق، غير مائل فيما أنا عليه من المذهب إلى الهوى والشهوة، عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك. ثم إن أشعريا آخر حكى هذا كله في تصنيفه وشنع بذلك علينا وقاتل بسلاح غيره، ثم إن هذا الأشعري الثاني لم يرض بهذا التشنيع حتى ذكر هذه المسألة في كتابه في بيان أقاويل القائلين بقدم العالم، وذكر مقالة ابن قلس، أن العالم قديم لأن سبب خلق العالم جود الباري جل وعلا، وجوده قديم فيكون العالم قديما ثم قال: وقريب من مذهب ابن قلس قول طائفة من الناس، وحكى هذه المقالة التي نحن عليها. ثم إن أشعريا آخر اشتغل بإبطال هذه المقالة فاستدل على ذلك بأن الكفر فعل العبد وهو مفعوله فإذا كان الفعل والمفعول واحدا قال فإن قالوا الكفر ليس بمفعول للكافر، قلنا له: فإذا لا يكون فعل الكافر لوقوع الاتفاق على أن ما لا يكون مفعولا للعبد لا يكون قد فعله العبد. قال: وإن قالوا: الكفر ليس بفعل العبد ولا بمفعول له، قيل: قد خرج الكفر عن أن يكون بالعبد والتحقتم بالجبرية، وصار الكافر معذورا. قال ولو قال الكفر مفعول العبد وليس بفعل له. قيل لهم فيكون الكفر إذا من فعل الله تعالى، ويلزمهم على هذا خروج قدرة العبد عن أن يكون لها معنى، لأن الكفر إذا كان مفعولا للعبد بفعل قد خلقه الله تعالى فلا معنى لقدرة العبد، ولا فائدة لها ولا معنى لقولهم: إن الكافر يقدر أن يكفر. فهذا ما رأيته من شبه الأشعرية في قولهم: إن الخلق والمخلوق واحد.
And an Ash'ari spoke about all this; I narrated it in his words lest it be thought that I am deficient in reporting the specious arguments of the opponents. And the narration is in a manner that facilitates the answer, rather one knows that the follower of the proof is submissive to the truth, not inclined, regarding what I am upon of the school, to caprice and desire. May God protect us from that. Then another Ash'ari narrated all this in his book and disparaged us thereby and fought with the weapon of others. Then this second Ash'ari was not satisfied with this disparagement until he mentioned this issue in his book in the exposition of the statements of those who assert the eternity of the world, and he mentioned the statement of Ibn Qals: that the world is eternal because the cause of the creation of the world is the generosity of the Creator, may He be exalted, and His generosity is eternal, so the world is eternal. Then he said: And close to the school of Ibn Qals is the statement of a group of people, and he narrated this statement which we are upon. Then another Ash'ari occupied himself with refuting this statement, and he adduced evidence for that by saying that unbelief is the act of the servant and it is his object. So if the act and the object are one, he said: If they say: unbelief is not an object for the unbeliever, we say to him: Then it would not be an act of the unbeliever because of the agreement that whatever is not an object of the servant is not something the servant has done. He said: And if they say: unbelief is neither an act of the servant nor an object for him, it is said: Then unbelief has exited from being by the servant, and you have joined the Jabriyya, and the unbeliever becomes excused. He said: And if they say: unbelief is an object of the servant but not an act of his. It is said to them: Then unbelief would be from the act of God, and it follows from this the removal of the servant's power from having any meaning, because if unbelief is an object of the servant by an act that God has created, then there is no meaning to the servant's power, nor any benefit to it, nor any meaning to their statement: The unbeliever is able to commit unbelief. This is what I have seen of the specious arguments of the Ash'ariyya in their statement that creation and the created are one.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage begins with the author defending his method of quoting opponents verbatim, emphasizing that he does so to fully present their objections and then refute them, demonstrating that his commitment is to the truth, not sectarian bias. He then reports that another Ash'ari writer included the very argument under discussion in his own book, linking it to the claim of the eternity of the world (citing Ibn Qals: the cause of creation is God's eternal generosity, so the world must be eternal). This Ash'ari used this to discredit the Maturidi position by association. Next, a third Ash'ari tried to refute the Maturidi position directly, focusing on the relationship between act (fi'l) and object (maf'ul). He argues that unbelief (kufr) is both the act of the servant and his object. If act and object are one, then any attempt to separate them leads to contradictions: if kufr is not the servant's object, it cannot be his act; if it is neither his act nor object, then he is not responsible, which leads to Jabri determinism; if it is his object but not his act, then it becomes God's act, nullifying the servant's power. The author presents these as the specious arguments (shubah) of the Ash'ariyya in their claim that khalq and makhluq are identical, implying that their reasoning forces them into contradictions.

Key Terms
شبه (shubah)
أشعري (Ash'ari)
قدم العالم (qidam al-‘ālam)
جود (jūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quoting an opponent's argument verbatim indicates weakness or concession.
Correction: The author explains that doing so shows he is a follower of proof, not bias, and it actually facilitates a clear answer.

Checkpoint
Why does the author quote the first Ash'ari verbatim?
A) To mock him
B) To show he is not deficient in reporting arguments and to demonstrate his own commitment to truth
C) To avoid copyright issues
D) To confuse the reader

Key Takeaway
The author's method of quoting opponents fully underscores his commitment to evidence-based argumentation, while the second Ash'ari's tactic shows how theological positions can be unfairly associated with extreme views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you discuss matters of faith with others, do you accurately represent their views, even if it makes your own position harder to defend? What would it cost you to commit to the author's method of quoting opponents verbatim?
?
The second Ash'ari fought with the weapon of others—arguments he may not have fully owned. Are there beliefs you hold simply because they come from a trusted authority, without having examined the evidence yourself? How can you move from borrowed conviction to personal certitude?
?
The author describes the follower of proof as 'submissive to the truth, not inclined to caprice and desire.' In what area of your life are you most tempted to twist the truth to suit your comfort or reputation? What specific step will you take today to align yourself with the truth, even if it is uncomfortable?

Faith and Disbelief

Act and Object: The Ash'ari Argument on Kufr
Third Ash'ari's argument: kufr as act and object, three possible objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that unbelief (kufr) is both the act and object of the servant, and how it leads to contradictions if act and object are separated.

Core Idea
If act and object are one, separating them forces either determinism or divine injustice.

Here we examine the third Ash'ari's direct refutation of the Maturidi position that khalq and makhluq are distinct. He argues that kufr is both the act (fi'l) and object (maf'ul) of the servant, and then explores three possible ways to separate them, each leading to an unacceptable consequence. This passage is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise
The Ash'ari states that kufr is the act of the servant and his object, so act and object are identical. Note this starting claim.
The Ash'ari takes the Maturidi distinction between act and object as false.
2
Follow the three cases
The Ash'ari presents three if-then scenarios: (1) kufr is not an object → not an act; (2) kufr is neither act nor object → Jabri determinism; (3) kufr is object but not act → God's act, nullifying human power.
Each scenario forces a theological problem.
3
Consider the conclusion
The Ash'ari closes by saying these are the specious arguments of the Ash'ariyya in claiming creation and created are one. Reflect on how this argument is meant to refute the Maturidi view.
The author does not give his response here, but presents the objections for analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتكلم بهذا كله بعض الأشعرية حكيته بلفظه لئلا يظن بي التقصير في حكاية شبه الخصوم. والحكاية على وجه يسهل على الجواب، بل يعلم أن متبع الدليل منقاد للحق، غير مائل فيما أنا عليه من المذهب إلى الهوى والشهوة، عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك. ثم إن أشعريا آخر حكى هذا كله في تصنيفه وشنع بذلك علينا وقاتل بسلاح غيره، ثم إن هذا الأشعري الثاني لم يرض بهذا التشنيع حتى ذكر هذه المسألة في كتابه في بيان أقاويل القائلين بقدم العالم، وذكر مقالة ابن قلس، أن العالم قديم لأن سبب خلق العالم جود الباري جل وعلا، وجوده قديم فيكون العالم قديما ثم قال: وقريب من مذهب ابن قلس قول طائفة من الناس، وحكى هذه المقالة التي نحن عليها. ثم إن أشعريا آخر اشتغل بإبطال هذه المقالة فاستدل على ذلك بأن الكفر فعل العبد وهو مفعوله فإذا كان الفعل والمفعول واحدا قال فإن قالوا الكفر ليس بمفعول للكافر، قلنا له: فإذا لا يكون فعل الكافر لوقوع الاتفاق على أن ما لا يكون مفعولا للعبد لا يكون قد فعله العبد. قال: وإن قالوا: الكفر ليس بفعل العبد ولا بمفعول له، قيل: قد خرج الكفر عن أن يكون بالعبد والتحقتم بالجبرية، وصار الكافر معذورا. قال ولو قال الكفر مفعول العبد وليس بفعل له. قيل لهم فيكون الكفر إذا من فعل الله تعالى، ويلزمهم على هذا خروج قدرة العبد عن أن يكون لها معنى، لأن الكفر إذا كان مفعولا للعبد بفعل قد خلقه الله تعالى فلا معنى لقدرة العبد، ولا فائدة لها ولا معنى لقولهم: إن الكافر يقدر أن يكفر. فهذا ما رأيته من شبه الأشعرية في قولهم: إن الخلق والمخلوق واحد.
And an Ash'ari spoke about all this; I narrated it in his words lest it be thought that I am deficient in reporting the specious arguments of the opponents. And the narration is in a manner that facilitates the answer, rather one knows that the follower of the proof is submissive to the truth, not inclined, regarding what I am upon of the school, to caprice and desire. May God protect us from that. Then another Ash'ari narrated all this in his book and disparaged us thereby and fought with the weapon of others. Then this second Ash'ari was not satisfied with this disparagement until he mentioned this issue in his book in the exposition of the statements of those who assert the eternity of the world, and he mentioned the statement of Ibn Qals: that the world is eternal because the cause of the creation of the world is the generosity of the Creator, may He be exalted, and His generosity is eternal, so the world is eternal. Then he said: And close to the school of Ibn Qals is the statement of a group of people, and he narrated this statement which we are upon. Then another Ash'ari occupied himself with refuting this statement, and he adduced evidence for that by saying that unbelief is the act of the servant and it is his object. So if the act and the object are one, he said: If they say: unbelief is not an object for the unbeliever, we say to him: Then it would not be an act of the unbeliever because of the agreement that whatever is not an object of the servant is not something the servant has done. He said: And if they say: unbelief is neither an act of the servant nor an object for him, it is said: Then unbelief has exited from being by the servant, and you have joined the Jabriyya, and the unbeliever becomes excused. He said: And if they say: unbelief is an object of the servant but not an act of his. It is said to them: Then unbelief would be from the act of God, and it follows from this the removal of the servant's power from having any meaning, because if unbelief is an object of the servant by an act that God has created, then there is no meaning to the servant's power, nor any benefit to it, nor any meaning to their statement: The unbeliever is able to commit unbelief. This is what I have seen of the specious arguments of the Ash'ariyya in their statement that creation and the created are one.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage begins with the author defending his method of quoting opponents verbatim, emphasizing that he does so to fully present their objections and then refute them, demonstrating that his commitment is to the truth, not sectarian bias. He then reports that another Ash'ari writer included the very argument under discussion in his own book, linking it to the claim of the eternity of the world (citing Ibn Qals: the cause of creation is God's eternal generosity, so the world must be eternal). This Ash'ari used this to discredit the Maturidi position by association. Next, a third Ash'ari tried to refute the Maturidi position directly, focusing on the relationship between act (fi'l) and object (maf'ul). He argues that unbelief (kufr) is both the act of the servant and his object. If act and object are one, then any attempt to separate them leads to contradictions: if kufr is not the servant's object, it cannot be his act; if it is neither his act nor object, then he is not responsible, which leads to Jabri determinism; if it is his object but not his act, then it becomes God's act, nullifying the servant's power. The author presents these as the specious arguments (shubah) of the Ash'ariyya in their claim that khalq and makhluq are identical, implying that their reasoning forces them into contradictions.

Key Terms
كفر (kufr)
فعل (fi'l)
مفعول (maf'ul)
جبرية (Jabriyya)
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari argument proves that kufr must be both act and object, which supports their position.
Correction: The author presents this as a specious argument (shubha) that forces contradictions; it is not a valid refutation but a reductio that shows the problems with claiming act and object are identical.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari argument, what happens if one says kufr is an object of the servant but not his act?
A) The servant is fully responsible.
B) Kufr becomes God's act, nullifying the servant's power.
C) The servant is excused.
D) The act and object remain identical.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument forces a choice between contradictory theological positions, showing that claiming act and object are identical leads to inevitable problems.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that if kufr is not the servant's act, the unbeliever is excused. Have you ever excused someone's disbelief because of their upbringing or circumstances? What does that reveal about your view of divine justice and human responsibility? Will you continue to excuse them, or will you hold them accountable as the argument demands?
?
The third objection claims that separating act and object nullifies human power. When you sin, do you sometimes feel that it was ‘destined’ or ‘beyond your control’? How does this argument challenge that feeling? Are you ready to take full ownership of your actions as your own, without attributing them to God's creation?
?
The Ash'ari forces a choice: either kufr is fully the servant's act and object, or it leads to determinism and excuses the sinner. Do you live as though your good deeds are from God but your sins are your own? Is that consistent? What would it mean for you to accept that both obedience and disobedience are your acts, fully your responsibility before God?

Distinguishing Act from Effect in Kufr
Response to Ash'ari argument that kufr as act and effect collapses distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi distinction between an act (fi'l) and an effect (maf'ul) in the context of human actions and divine creation, and why kufr is the servant's act but God's effect.

Core Idea
What we do is our act, but only God creates the effect.

This passage addresses a subtle objection from Ash'ari theologians: if disbelief (kufr) is both the act of the servant and an effect (created by God), then the distinction between act and effect collapses. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, offers a crisp rebuttal, clarifying that an act and its created reality are distinct categories. Let's dive into this key Maturidi defense of human responsibility without compromising divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the Ash'ari objection stated at the beginning. Understand the logic: if kufr is the servant's act (fi'l) and also an effect (maf'ul) of God, then act and effect are the same thing.
2
Identify the author's response
The author rejects the premise. He says kufr is the servant's act but not his effect; it is God's effect. Notice the distinction: act is what the servant does; effect is what God creates.
The key sentence: 'الكفر فعله لا مفعول وهو مفعول الله تعالى'.
3
Examine the refutation of the consensus claim
The opponent claims consensus that if something is not the servant's effect, it cannot be his act. The author denies this consensus. He also reminds that he has proven that the mode of being (wujub) of act and effect are different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة أخرى والرد عليها] وأما ما قال غيره من الأشعرية : إن الكفر فعل العبد وهو مفعول فدل ذلك على جواز كون الفعل مفعولا فالجواب أن هذا غير مسلم بل الكفر فعله لا مفعول وهو مفعول الله تعالى، ومن ادعى من الاتفاق أن ما ليس بمفعول للعبد ليس بفعل له، باطل وخصومه لا يوافقونه في ذلك، وقد أقمنا الدلالة على كون وجوب الفعل غير المفعول.
Another doubt and the response to it: As for what another of the Ash'aris said: 'Kufr is the act of the servant and it is an effect, so that indicates the permissibility of the act being an effect.' The answer is that this is not accepted; rather, kufr is his act, not his effect, and it is the effect of God Most High. And whoever claims from consensus that what is not an effect of the servant is not an act of his, it is false, and his opponents do not agree with him in that. And we have established the proof that the ontological status of the act is other than the effect.
Source pages p. 542

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Ash'ari opponent argues that since kufr (disbelief) is the act of the human servant and also an effect (created by God), it follows that the same thing can be both act and effect, blurring the distinction. The author rejects this: kufr is indeed the act of the servant, but it is not his effect (the servant does not create it); it is the effect of God alone. He denies the alleged consensus that if something is not an effect of the servant, it cannot be his act. The author also states that he has already established that the mode of being (wujub) of an act is different from that of an effect, preserving the distinction.

Key Terms
الكفر
الفعل
المفعول
العبد
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an action is attributed to a human, then the human must be the creator of that action.
Correction: The Maturidi position is that the human performs the act (fi'l) but the act's created existence (maf'ul) comes from God alone.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of kufr?
A) It is the act and effect of the servant
B) It is the act of the servant and the effect of God
C) It is the act of God and the effect of the servant
D) It is neither act nor effect of anyone

Key Takeaway
Kufr is the servant's act (fi'l) but God's effect (maf'ul), preserving human responsibility without attributing creation to the servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your actions, even your sins like kufr, are your responsibility and not excuse them as 'God made me do it'? How does this distinction between act and effect change the way you own your faults?
?
When you disobey, do you attribute the act to yourself and the existence to God? Reflect on a specific sin: can you honestly say it was your act but permitted by God? How does that affect your repentance?
?
If kufr is the servant's act but God's effect, what does that imply about your own faith? Are you actively choosing to believe, or do you passively wait for God to create belief in you? How does this lesson push you to take responsibility for your iman?

Benefit and Motive in Human Actions
Nasafi refutes the analogy between human and divine actions regarding benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human actions cannot be directly analogized to God's actions, due to the inherent self-interest in human acts.

Core Idea
Human actions always involve self-interest; God's actions do not.

Imam Nasafi engages with an objection: if a person does something that benefits others but not himself, is that foolish? The opponents say no, giving examples of altruism. Nasafi responds by showing that such actions are still motivated by the agent's own benefit—praise and reward. This reveals a critical flaw in analogizing God's acts to human acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow come through.
Notice the dialectical structure: claim, counterclaim, examples, rebuttal.
2
Identify the opponents' claim and examples
The opponents claim that actions benefiting others are not foolish even if the agent gets no direct benefit. They give examples: feeding the hungry, clothing the naked, building a fortress for travelers.
Write down these examples in your own words.
3
Analyze Nasafi's rebuttal
Nasafi argues that these actions are not considered foolish because the agent gains praise and reward. He provides a conditional scenario: if the agent knew no one would know and no reward would come, the same act would be foolish.
This conditional scenario proves the point that self-interest is the actual motive.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since God cannot benefit from praise or reward, the analogy between human acts and divine acts is invalid. This principle extends to commands and prohibitions.
Reflect on why this argument is important for understanding God's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : دعواكم أن في الشاهد من يفعل فعلاً لا ينتفع هو به لم يكن سفها إذ كان ينتفع به غيره قلنا: ليس كذلك بل يعد سفها. فلم قلتم ذلك. فإن قالوا: من أطعم جائعًا أو كسا عاريًا لا يعد سفيها وإن كان لا ينتفع هو به، لما أن غيره ينتفع به وهو الجائع العاري، وكذا من بنى في مفازة من المفاوز رباطاً لا يعد سفيها، وإن كان من قصده أن لا يسكن هو لما أن غيره من المحتاجين ينتفعون به. قيل: إنما لم يعد هذا سفهًا لا لمكان انتفاع غيره بل لمكان انتفاع نفسه فإنه ينال به الثناء الحسن في الدنيا والثواب الموعود في الآخرة، حتى إنه لو بنى مع علمه أن أحدًا من الناس لا يعلم أنه هو الباني فلا يحصل له الثناء، وأخبره نبي من الأنبياء أنه لا ينال الثواب بذلك، ثم بنى مع هذا وأنفق فيه الأموال مع علمه أنه لا حمد له ولا أجر بذلك فإنه يعد سفيها، وإن كان يعلم أن غيره ينتفع به. والله تعالى أنه لا ينتفع بفعله لما مر من استحالة الانتفاع عليه لا يكون سفها، فدل أن اعتبار الغائب في هذا بالشاهد فاسد . فكذا في الأمر والنهي والتكليف.
Then we say: Your claim that in the witnessed, whoever does an act from which he does not benefit is not foolish, since another benefits from it, we say: It is not so; rather he is counted as foolish. So why did you say that? If they say: Whoever feeds a hungry person or clothes a naked person is not counted as foolish, even if he does not benefit from it, because another benefits from it, namely the hungry and naked; and likewise, whoever builds a fortress in a wasteland is not counted as foolish, even if he intends not to dwell in it, because others in need benefit from it. It was said: This was not considered foolish not because of the benefit to others, but because of the benefit to himself, for he attains through it good praise in this world and the promised reward in the Hereafter. Indeed, if he built while knowing that no one among people knows that he is the builder, so he obtains no praise, and a prophet of the prophets informed him that he would not attain reward for that, then he built with this and spent wealth while knowing that there is no praise or reward for it, then he would be counted as foolish, even if he knows that others benefit from it. And God—exalted is He—does not benefit from His act, because of the aforementioned impossibility of benefit for Him, He is not foolish. So it is clear that considering the absent through the witnessed in this is corrupt. Likewise in the matter of command and prohibition and obligation.
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage addresses the opponents' claim that in the witnessed world, an action from which the agent himself does not benefit is still not foolish if another benefits. The responder disagrees and says it can be foolish. The opponents give examples: feeding a hungry person or building a fortress for travelers. The responder explains that such actions are not considered foolish because the agent benefits from praise and reward. To prove this, he describes a scenario where an agent builds despite knowing that no one will know and no reward will come—such an agent is indeed considered foolish, even though others benefit. Since God cannot benefit from any reward or praise, His actions cannot be analogized to human actions. The analogy between witnessed and absent is therefore invalid. This reasoning extends to divine commands and prohibitions.

Key Terms
سفه
ثناء
ثواب
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Altruistic actions prove that humans sometimes act without any self-interest.
Correction: Nasafi shows that even altruistic actions are motivated by the agent's desire for praise or reward.

Checkpoint
According to the responder, what is the actual reason a human is not called foolish when feeding the hungry?
A) He benefits the other person.
B) He gains praise and reward.
C) He acts out of compassion.
D) He fulfills a religious obligation.

Key Takeaway
Human actions are always tied to some personal benefit, so God's actions cannot be judged by the same standard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you truly seek only God's reward, or do you also secretly hope for recognition from people? Imagine you knew for certain that no one would ever know—would you still do it? Would your faith sustain that silence?
?
If God does not need your worship or obedience—since He does not benefit from it—then why do you worship? Is it merely to avoid punishment and gain reward, or have you tasted something deeper that frees you from seeing your acts as transactions?
?
This lesson shows that even the most altruistic human actions are tied to some personal gain. Does that disturb you? Can you think of an act you did with absolutely no expectation—not even from God—and if not, what does that say about the purity of your intention?

The Impossibility of Fully Thanking God
Nasafi proves the impossibility of fully thanking God due to infinite regress of gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human beings cannot fully thank God for even a single blessing, because the act of gratitude itself requires further divine assistance.

Core Idea
Every act of gratitude is itself a new blessing, leading to an infinite regress.

Nasafi begins by pointing out a profound dilemma: the intellect itself is a gift from God, so it cannot encompass all His blessings. He then demonstrates that any attempt to thank God for a blessing requires further divine assistance, creating an endless chain. This shows that reason alone is insufficient for fulfilling the obligation of gratitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the rhetorical questions and logical flow.
2
Identify the initial premise
The author states that the intellect is part of God's blessings and cannot encompass the whole. This is the foundation.
3
Trace the infinite regress of gratitude
The author explains that each act of gratitude requires tawfīq, which is itself a new blessing demanding more gratitude, ad infinitum.
4
Understand the conclusion
Even one blessing cannot be fully thanked, let alone all blessings. This shows the insufficiency of reason alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه طول عمره ومدة حياته؟! وكيف يعرف ذلك ؟! والعقل الذي به نتوصل إلى معرفة الأشياء جزء من أجزاء نعمه والجزء لا يحيط بالكل الذي هو جزء منه. وإذا كان الأمر على ما قررنا لن يتصور من العبد أن يأتي بشكر يكافئ أدنى نعمة من النعم فضلاً عن أن يكون مكافئًا لما تقلب هو فيه من نعمه في جميع عمره. يحققه أنه لا يتمكن من أداء شكر نعمه إلا بتوفيق من الله وتيسير منه على ذلك، وذلك نعمة جديدة يجب بمقابلتها الشكر ، ولا يتوصل إلى شكرها إلا بأثر آخر للتوفيق، وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى. فإذا على هذه القضية لا يمكن للعبد الخروج عن شكر نعمة واحدة وإن استغرق جميع عمره لشكرها، فكيف يمكنه إذا شكر جميع نعمه؟! والشكر واجب عليه فلابد من بيان قدر الشكر الذي يستوجبه الله تعالى على عباده على نعمه ليتمكنوا من أداء ذلك. وكذا العقول قاصرة عن كيفية شكر كل نعمة كما هي عاجزة عن الوقوف على كميته وكذا هي قاصرة عن الوقوف على حسن الشكر ودقته فلابد من بيان سمعي يرد بذلك كله ليمكن للعباد التقصي عما لزمهم من عهدة تكليف أداء الشكر أن الحكيم إذا أمر عبدًا من عبيده بشيء لابد من يبين ذلك بيانا يتمكن العبد من الائتمار إذ لولا البيان لكان ذلك تكليف ما ليس في وسع المكلف الائتمار له وهو خارج عن الحكمة.
قول البراهمة باطل]
وبهذا يبطل قول البراهمة القائلين بوقوع الغنية لوقوف العقل على المحاسن والقبائح، وحصول الأمر والنهي بها عن الأوامر الشرعية، وكون العقول كافية للتكليف إذ بينا أن لا كفاية البتة بذلك على ما قررنا من قصور العقول عن الوقوف على ما بينا.
…upon him his entire life and the duration of his life?! And how does he know that?! The intellect by which we arrive at knowledge of things is part of the parts of His blessings, and the part does not encompass the whole of which it is a part. If the matter is as we have established, it is inconceivable that the servant should come with a gratitude that equals the least of blessings, let alone that it equals what he is immersed in of His blessings throughout his entire life. He proves that he is unable to perform gratitude for His blessings except by success (tawfīq) from God and facilitation from Him upon that, and that is a new blessing for which gratitude is obligatory in return, and he cannot attain gratitude for it except by another effect of success, and so on ad infinitum. Thus, according to this proposition, the servant cannot exit from gratitude for even one blessing even if he spends his entire life thanking it, so how can he then thank all of his blessings?! Gratitude is obligatory upon him, so there must be an explanation of the amount of gratitude that God Almighty warrants upon His servants for His blessings so that they may perform that. Likewise, intellects are incapable of the manner (kayfiyya) of gratitude for every blessing just as they are incapable of knowing its quantity, and likewise they are incapable of knowing the goodness (ḥusn) and exactness of gratitude, so there must be a scriptural explanation (bayān samʿī) that conveys all of that so that the servants can fulfill what is incumbent upon them from the obligation of performing gratitude. The wise one, if he commands a servant of his servants with something, he must clarify it in a clarification that enables the servant to obey, for if there were no clarification, that would be imposing an obligation beyond the capacity of the obligated to obey, and that is outside of wisdom.
The saying of the Brahmins is false]
And with this, the saying of the Brahmins is refuted, who claim self-sufficiency (al-ghunya) because the intellect can know good and evil, and that commands and prohibitions occur through it apart from scriptural commands, and that intellects are sufficient for obligation, for we have shown that there is no sufficiency at all in that according to what we established of the incapacity of intellects to know what we have explained.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It begins by arguing that a person cannot fully thank God for even a single blessing because the intellect itself is a blessing and cannot encompass the whole. Then it shows gratitude leads to an infinite regress since each act of gratitude requires further divine assistance. Therefore, reason alone cannot determine the proper manner of gratitude. The passage then refutes the Brahmins who claimed reason is sufficient for moral knowledge and obligation. The author concludes that intellects are incapable of such knowledge, so scriptural guidance is necessary.

Key Terms
شكر
نعمة
توفيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can fully thank God for His blessings through one's own efforts without divine assistance.
Correction: The passage shows that each act of gratitude requires further divine tawfīq, making it impossible to ever complete gratitude independently.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that a servant cannot adequately thank God for even one blessing?
A) Because the servant is sinful and unworthy.
B) Because each act of gratitude is itself a new blessing requiring further gratitude, leading to infinite regress.
C) Because God does not require gratitude.
D) Because the intellect can only understand worldly matters.

Key Takeaway
Human gratitude depends on divine tawfīq, and no finite act can fully repay infinite blessings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank God, do you ever consider that your very ability to thank is itself a blessing you didn't earn? How does that change your understanding of gratitude?
?
If you cannot fully thank God for even one blessing, what does that say about your daily assumption that you can repay Him through good deeds?
?
The infinite regress of gratitude shows your utter dependence on God. Does this make you feel humbled or anxious? How should it change the way you begin your prayers?
