The Necessity of Scriptural Guidance and Refutation of the Brahmins
Nasafi argues for the necessity of revelation in determining gratitude and refutes the Brahmins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scriptural guidance is necessary for the obligation of gratitude and how this refutes the Brahmins' claim of intellectual self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Intellects are incapable of knowing the quantity, quality, and moral value of gratitude; revelation is essential.

After establishing the impossibility of fully thanking God by reason alone, Nasafi argues that the obligation of gratitude requires a divine clarification of its manner and amount. He then dismisses the Brahmins' doctrine that reason suffices for moral knowledge and obligation, showing that intellects are inherently limited.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the three areas where intellect is said to be incapable.
2
Identify the three incapacities of intellect
The passage states intellect is incapable of knowing the quantity (kammiyya), manner (kayfiyya), and goodness/refinement (ḥusn and diqqa) of gratitude.
3
Understand the need for scriptural explanation
Because of these incapacities, a scriptural explanation (bayān samʿī) is necessary for the servant to fulfill the obligation.
4
See the refutation of the Brahmins
The Brahmins claimed self-sufficiency of reason, but the earlier arguments show reason's limitations, thus refuting their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه طول عمره ومدة حياته؟! وكيف يعرف ذلك ؟! والعقل الذي به نتوصل إلى معرفة الأشياء جزء من أجزاء نعمه والجزء لا يحيط بالكل الذي هو جزء منه. وإذا كان الأمر على ما قررنا لن يتصور من العبد أن يأتي بشكر يكافئ أدنى نعمة من النعم فضلاً عن أن يكون مكافئًا لما تقلب هو فيه من نعمه في جميع عمره. يحققه أنه لا يتمكن من أداء شكر نعمه إلا بتوفيق من الله وتيسير منه على ذلك، وذلك نعمة جديدة يجب بمقابلتها الشكر ، ولا يتوصل إلى شكرها إلا بأثر آخر للتوفيق، وهكذا إلى ما لا يتناهى. فإذا على هذه القضية لا يمكن للعبد الخروج عن شكر نعمة واحدة وإن استغرق جميع عمره لشكرها، فكيف يمكنه إذا شكر جميع نعمه؟! والشكر واجب عليه فلابد من بيان قدر الشكر الذي يستوجبه الله تعالى على عباده على نعمه ليتمكنوا من أداء ذلك. وكذا العقول قاصرة عن كيفية شكر كل نعمة كما هي عاجزة عن الوقوف على كميته وكذا هي قاصرة عن الوقوف على حسن الشكر ودقته فلابد من بيان سمعي يرد بذلك كله ليمكن للعباد التقصي عما لزمهم من عهدة تكليف أداء الشكر أن الحكيم إذا أمر عبدًا من عبيده بشيء لابد من يبين ذلك بيانا يتمكن العبد من الائتمار إذ لولا البيان لكان ذلك تكليف ما ليس في وسع المكلف الائتمار له وهو خارج عن الحكمة.
قول البراهمة باطل]
وبهذا يبطل قول البراهمة القائلين بوقوع الغنية لوقوف العقل على المحاسن والقبائح، وحصول الأمر والنهي بها عن الأوامر الشرعية، وكون العقول كافية للتكليف إذ بينا أن لا كفاية البتة بذلك على ما قررنا من قصور العقول عن الوقوف على ما بينا.
…upon him his entire life and the duration of his life?! And how does he know that?! The intellect by which we arrive at knowledge of things is part of the parts of His blessings, and the part does not encompass the whole of which it is a part. If the matter is as we have established, it is inconceivable that the servant should come with a gratitude that equals the least of blessings, let alone that it equals what he is immersed in of His blessings throughout his entire life. He proves that he is unable to perform gratitude for His blessings except by success (tawfīq) from God and facilitation from Him upon that, and that is a new blessing for which gratitude is obligatory in return, and he cannot attain gratitude for it except by another effect of success, and so on ad infinitum. Thus, according to this proposition, the servant cannot exit from gratitude for even one blessing even if he spends his entire life thanking it, so how can he then thank all of his blessings?! Gratitude is obligatory upon him, so there must be an explanation of the amount of gratitude that God Almighty warrants upon His servants for His blessings so that they may perform that. Likewise, intellects are incapable of the manner (kayfiyya) of gratitude for every blessing just as they are incapable of knowing its quantity, and likewise they are incapable of knowing the goodness (ḥusn) and exactness of gratitude, so there must be a scriptural explanation (bayān samʿī) that conveys all of that so that the servants can fulfill what is incumbent upon them from the obligation of performing gratitude. The wise one, if he commands a servant of his servants with something, he must clarify it in a clarification that enables the servant to obey, for if there were no clarification, that would be imposing an obligation beyond the capacity of the obligated to obey, and that is outside of wisdom.
The saying of the Brahmins is false]
And with this, the saying of the Brahmins is refuted, who claim self-sufficiency (al-ghunya) because the intellect can know good and evil, and that commands and prohibitions occur through it apart from scriptural commands, and that intellects are sufficient for obligation, for we have shown that there is no sufficiency at all in that according to what we established of the incapacity of intellects to know what we have explained.
Source pages p. 684

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. It begins by arguing that a person cannot fully thank God for even a single blessing because the intellect itself is a blessing and cannot encompass the whole. Then it shows gratitude leads to an infinite regress since each act of gratitude requires further divine assistance. Therefore, reason alone cannot determine the proper manner of gratitude. The passage then refutes the Brahmins who claimed reason is sufficient for moral knowledge and obligation. The author concludes that intellects are incapable of such knowledge, so scriptural guidance is necessary.

Key Terms
بيان سمعي
عقل
براهمة
غنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The human intellect alone can determine all moral obligations without needing divine revelation.
Correction: The passage argues that intellect is incapable of knowing the quantity, manner, and goodness of gratitude, so revelation is indispensable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three aspects of gratitude that the intellect cannot know?
A) The time, place, and person.
B) The quantity, manner, and goodness/exactness.
C) The language, intention, and sincerity.
D) The reward, punishment, and duration.

Key Takeaway
Revelation is indispensable for understanding the obligation of gratitude; reason alone is insufficient for moral guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the blessings in your life, do you ever feel unsure about how to properly thank God? Where has your intellect been insufficient in guiding your gratitude, and have you turned to revelation for the answer?
?
The Brahmins thought their intellect was enough. In what area of your life are you most tempted to rely on your own reasoning over God’s clear commands? Will you continue that, or will you submit to scripture even when it contradicts your logic?
?
If God is the Wise Commander, why do you sometimes act as if His commands are unclear? Is there a specific command you have delayed obeying because you think you need more intellectual insight? What would it cost you to obey unconditionally today?

Understanding the Opponent's Accusation
Describes the opponent's charge that Ahl al-Sunna allow unmerited favor and child punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's accusation against Ahl al-Sunna regarding unmerited favor and child punishment, and why it stems from a misunderstanding of divine attributes.

Core Idea
The opponent condemns Ahl al-Sunna for affirming Allah's absolute freedom.

This passage presents a key accusation leveled against Ahl al-Sunna by their opponents. The critic claims that believing Allah can give unmerited favor or cause pain to children without sin implies divine injustice. Before we can respond, we must first grasp exactly what the opponent is arguing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the strong language: the opponent declares Ahl al-Sunna disbelievers.
The passage uses the verb 'yukaffir'—declares unbelievers.
2
Identify the two accusations
The opponent cites two beliefs of Ahl al-Sunna: (1) Allah can give favor without merit, (2) Allah can cause pain to children without sin and still compensate. Write them down.
3
Understand the opponent's reasoning
Why does the opponent consider these beliefs kufr? He thinks unmerited favor and child pain are morally evil (qabih) and thus cannot be attributed to Allah.
The opponent assumes a standard of justice that limits Allah's actions.
4
Prepare for the refutation
Now that you know the accusation, consider: what assumptions does the opponent make about Allah's nature? How might Ahl al-Sunna respond?
The refutation will come in later passages.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن هذا رجل يكفر أهل السنة والجماعة وينسبهم إلى الضلال والبدعة بقولهم: إن الله تعالى ليس بمحجور عن الإفضال إلى عباده، وهو إعطاء ما لم يستحقوا عليه بأعمالهم، وبقولهم: إن الله تعالى إن يؤلم الأطفال من غیر استحقاق بفعل قبيح (۸) واشتراط عوض وإن كان ذلك الإفضال بشيء قليل لا دوام له ولا ثبات والإيلام بألم يسير في مدة قصيرة.
Then indeed this man declares Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamaa to be disbelievers and attributes to them misguidance and heresy for their saying: 'Allah is not prevented from bestowing favor upon His servants, and that is giving them what they do not deserve by their acts,' and for their saying: 'Allah may cause pain to children without deserving by an evil act (8) and stipulating compensation even if that favor is something slight with no permanence or stability, and causing pain with slight pain for a short period.'
Source pages p. 831

Commentary
This passage describes the opponent's accusation against Ahl al-Sunna. The opponent declares Ahl al-Sunna disbelievers and accuses them of heresy because they hold two positions: (1) that Allah is not prevented from bestowing favor upon His servants even if they do not deserve it by their actions—this is divine bounty (tatawwul); (2) that Allah may cause pain to children without them having committed any evil act, and even stipulate compensation for that pain, even if the favor is slight and temporary. The opponent considers causing pain to children without sin to be evil (qabih) and thus Allah would not do it. The author presents this as the opponent's accusation without yet refuting it (the refutation may follow later).

Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm Allah's absolute sovereignty and wisdom; He is not bound by human concepts of justice but acts according to His will and wisdom.

B) It implies children have no souls
C) It contradicts God's mercy
D) It denies resurrection

Critique of Redundant Punishment for Abandoning Prayer
Critique of a view combining schools on punishment for abandoning prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of a view that combines different schools on the punishment for abandoning prayer and why positing an additional disliked act is redundant.

Core Idea
Abandoning prayer itself is the punishable act; no extra disliked act is needed.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi critiques a view that merges Hanafi and Mu'tazilite positions on the punishment for abandoning prayer. The author argues that if the threat of punishment already applies to the omission of the commanded act, introducing a separate hated (makruh) act as the basis for punishment is unnecessary and illogical. This critique defends the position that the act of abandonment itself is what warrants divine threat.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author’s critical tone as he dismantles the opposing view.
The passage starts with 'مذهب جميع [؟]' indicating a missing word, possibly 'أهل'. Keep that in mind.
2
Identify the two views
The author distinguishes between two positions: punishment due to the act of abandonment (view of Ahl al-Qibla) and punishment due to non-existence of the commanded act (view of Abu Hashim). The criticized view tries to combine both by adding a disliked act.
3
Understand the redundancy argument
The author points out that if the threat already applies to the omission itself, there is no need to posit an additional disliked act. This is the core logical flaw.
The author calls it 'one of the strangest opinions'.
4
Note the contradiction with Abu Hashim
The criticized scholar claimed to follow Abu Hashim but contradicted him by making the command a ruling in the opposite of the commanded act. Abu Hashim rejected that.
5
Conclude with consensus rejection
The author ends by stating that all Ahl al-Qibla reject this redundant view. This underscores the consensus that the abandonment itself is the punishable act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذهب جميع [؟] على تارك الصلاة لارتكابه ضدها المنهي عنه وهو الترك الذي هو فعل كما هو أهل القبلة. أما لانعدام ما أمر به من غير فعل ارتكبه كما هو مذهب هاشم. فإن كان متمسكا بمذاهب أهل السنة والجماعة فقال ما قال لرأي منح له غير معروض على قوانين الأصول، ولا مبني على قواعد المذهب، وإن كان مائلاً إلى مذهب القوم في مسألة الكلام وإلى مذهب أبي هاشم في هذه المسألة فقال ما قال بناء على رأيه، غير أنه مع هذا خالف أبا هاشم حيث جعل الأمر حكمًا في ضد المأمور به وهو الكراهية، وأبو هاشم أبى هذا. وهذا من أعجب الرأي؛ فإنه إن كان الوعيد متوجهًا على من انعدم المأمور من قبله وعوقب لذلك كما هو مذهب أبي هاشم، فأي حاجة إلى إثبات الكراهية في الضد، والوعيد بدونه متوجه والعقاب متحقق، وإن لم يكن بد من فعل محظور يرتكبه ووزر عظيم لحقه لتوجه الوعيد وتحقق العقوبة، وذلك هو فعل الترك، فكيف يزعم بتوجهه كل الوعيد الوارد لتارك الفرائض وثبوت العقوبة له لو لم يتغمده الله برحمته بمباشرة فعل مكروه ليس بمنهى عنه ولا محظور، وهذا مما يأباه جميع أهل القبلة.
The view of all [؟] concerning the one who abandons the prayer, because he commits its opposite which is prohibited, and that is the abandonment which is an act, as is the view of the people of the Qibla. But as for the non-existence of what was commanded without committing an act, as is the view of Hashim. If he is adhering to the schools of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, then what he said is an opinion granted to him not presented to the principles of usul, nor based on the rules of the school. And if he is inclined to the school of the people in the matter of kalam and to the school of Abu Hashim in this issue, then he said what he said based on his opinion, yet despite this he contradicted Abu Hashim in that he made the command a ruling in the opposite of the commanded act, which is karaha (disliked), and Abu Hashim refused this. And this is one of the strangest opinions: for if the threat is directed at the one from whom the commanded act is absent and he is punished for that as is the view of Abu Hashim, then what need is there to establish karaha in the opposite? The threat is directed without it and the punishment is realized. And if there must be a prohibited act that he commits and a great sin that befalls him for the threat to be directed and the punishment to be realized, that is the act of abandonment. So how does he claim that all the threats mentioned against those who abandon the obligatory duties and the establishment of punishment for him if God does not envelop him with His mercy, by committing a disliked act that is neither forbidden nor prohibited? And this is something that all the people of the Qibla reject.
Source pages p. 834

Commentary
This passage is a critique of an unnamed scholar who held a view that combines elements from Mu'tazilite and Hanafi positions on the punishment for abandoning prayer. The author begins by stating the view: the one who abandons prayer is punished because he commits the prohibited opposite, which is the act of abandonment. Then he examines whether the punishment is due to the non-existence of the commanded act (as Abu Hashim held) or due to committing a prohibited act. The author points out that if the threat is already directed at the omission itself, there is no need to posit an additional disliked (makruh) act as the cause of punishment. The scholar he critiques had introduced the concept of karaha in the opposite of the commanded act, contradicting Abu Hashim. The author finds this redundant and illogical, emphasizing that the abandonment itself is the prohibited act that warrants punishment. Therefore, the claim that punishment requires a makruh act is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla.

Key Terms
ترك
فعل
مأمور
كراهية
وعيد
عقوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that punishment for abandoning prayer requires a separate prohibited act beyond the omission itself.
Correction: The author shows that the omission itself is the prohibited act (because it is opposite of the commanded act), so no extra disliked or prohibited act is needed for punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the problem with the view that punishment for abandoning prayer requires a makruh act?
A) It contradicts the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna.
B) It is redundant because the threat already applies to the omission.
C) It is based on a weak hadith.
D) It was rejected by Abu Hashim.

Key Takeaway
The punishment for abandoning prayer is due to the act of omission itself, not an additional disliked act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you added extra requirements to your religious practice beyond what is prescribed, like the critic in this passage added karaha? What was your motivation, and how does this lesson call you to trust in Allah's mercy rather than your own additions?
?
Do you tend to complicate matters of worship with unnecessary details? Identify one area where you can simplify your practice to focus on the essentials commanded by Allah, not extra burdens. What will you do today to return to the basics?
?
Reflect on a time you judged someone for not observing a disliked act you considered important. How does the author's critique of redundant punishment correct your view? Will you change your attitude toward others' worship? What specific step will you take?

The Meaning of Power in Avoiding Sin
Nasafi distinguishes two meanings of power to answer an objection about avoiding sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's distinction between two meanings of power (qudra) and how it resolves the objection about avoiding sin while being capable.

Core Idea
Power can mean instrument capacity or act power; confusing them leads to impossible questions.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a tricky objection: Can someone avoid sin if they have the power to commit it? The answer depends entirely on what we mean by 'power.' He distinguishes two meanings and shows that the objector's question is only valid for one of them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the full passage once, focusing on the objection and Nasafi's response. Notice the two conditional statements.
The first condition uses 'if you mean... then yes'; the second uses 'if you mean... then impossible.'
2
Identify the two meanings of power
Identify the two definitions: power of instruments/causes and the true power of the act. Reflect on how they differ.
The first refers to ability outside of action; the second is identical with action.
3
Understand why one meaning makes the question impossible
Consider why asking about avoiding sin while having the power of the act is contradictory, because that power exists only with the act itself.
This prevents the objection from being meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها؟ فنقول: إن عنيت بالقدرة قدرة الآلات والأسباب فنعم، وإن عنيت حقيقة قدرة الفعل كان السؤال محالاً، لأن تلك القدرة توجد مع الفعل فكأنك قلت: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو فاعل لها وهذا محال. ثم يقابل بمثل هذا التشنيع فيقال: هل أعطى الله تعالى وليا قوة الطاعة بعين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، تمكنت وحشة عظيمة، وإن قال: نعم، أقر بقولنا، فإن قالوا: لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول: لو قيل له لم لا فعلت كذا؟ فيقول: لأنني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب. قلنا: هذا يكون عذرًا فيما منع عنه القدرة لا فيما ضيعها بإيثاره بدله.
As for their statement: 'Did anyone avoid the disobedience of God while being capable of it (qādir ʿalayhā)?' We say: If you mean by power (al-qudra) the power of instruments (ālāt) and causes (asbāb), then yes. If you mean the true power of the act (ḥaqīqat qudrat al-fiʿl), then the question is impossible, because that power exists with the act. It is as if you said: 'Did anyone avoid the disobedience of God while being a doer of it?' And this is impossible. Then it is countered with a similar objection, and it is said: Did God give a saint the power of obedience (quwwat al-ṭāʿa) identical to the obedience itself? If he says no, a great unease (waḥsha ʿaẓīma) arises. If he says yes, he affirms our statement. Then they say: There is no excuse for the servant in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) greater than his saying, when it is said to him 'Why did you not do such-and-such?' 'Because I was not able to do it.' So the like of it in the unseen (al-ghāʾib). We say: This is an excuse regarding what one is prevented from by power, not regarding what one wasted by preferring something else instead.
Source pages p. 847

Commentary
This passage handles an objection and a counter-objection. The objectors ask: Can someone avoid sin while having power over it? Nasafi answers: If by 'power' you mean the capacity of instruments and causes, then yes; if you mean the real power of the act itself, the question is impossible because that power exists only with the act—it would mean avoiding sin while doing it, a contradiction. Then he counters with a similar objection: Did God give a saint the power of obedience identical to the obedience? If they say no, a great unease arises; if yes, they agree with us. Then they argue that no excuse is greater in the witnessed world than saying 'I was not able,' and analogously in the unseen. Our response: That is an excuse only for what one is prevented from, not for what one wastes by preferring something else.

Key Terms
قدرة
آلات
أسباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that having the ability to sin means one must inevitably sin if they have power.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that the capacity of instruments does not necessitate the act; one can have the capacity and still avoid sin.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the term 'power' (qudra) refer to when asking if someone can avoid sin while being capable?
A) The power of the act itself
B) The capacity of instruments and causes
C) Divine power
D) Hypothetical power

Key Takeaway
The objection about avoiding sin while capable is only valid when power means instrumental capacity, not the power of the act itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rationalize a sin by thinking 'I had the power to stop but I didn't'? According to Nasafi, what kind of 'power' did you actually have in that moment? How does this distinction affect your accountability?
?
When you claim you are 'powerless' over a recurring sin, which meaning of power are you using? Are you ignoring the instrumental capacity Allah has given you to resist? What is one sin you will consciously avoid today using the capacity you already have?
?
If the power to sin only exists when you are sinning, then before the sin, your capacity to avoid it is a gift from Allah. How does this change your view of your own responsibility? What concrete step will you take this week to turn your capacity for obedience into actual obedience?

The Counter-Objection and the Proper Excuse of Inability
Nasafi counters with a dilemma about the power of obedience and refines the excuse of inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's counter-objection about the power of obedience and his refinement of the excuse of inability.

Core Idea
The excuse of inability only applies when one is prevented, not when one wastes the chance.

Nasafi now counters with a similar question: Did God give a saint the power of obedience identical to obedience? The answer leads to a dilemma. Then the objectors argue that in the visible world, 'I was not able' is a valid excuse, and analogously in the unseen. Nasafi responds by limiting the excuse to cases of genuine prevention, not neglect.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection
Read the passage from 'ثم يقابل' to the end. Notice the structure: a counter-objection, then an analogical argument, then Nasafi's response.
2
Understand the dilemma about power of obedience
Consider the question: Did God give the saint the power of obedience identical to obedience? Imagine both answers: saying no creates unease, saying yes confirms Nasafi's position.
This shows the opponent's difficulty.
3
Grasp the analogical argument from the witnessed world
The objectors say that in the visible world, claiming inability is a great excuse, so it should be the same in the unseen. Understand their reasoning.
They argue by analogy.
4
See Nasafi's distinction
Nasafi responds by distinguishing between being prevented from an act (where inability is an excuse) and wasting the capacity by choosing something else (where it is not).
This is the key point: the excuse only applies to prevention, not to misuse of ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها؟ فنقول: إن عنيت بالقدرة قدرة الآلات والأسباب فنعم، وإن عنيت حقيقة قدرة الفعل كان السؤال محالاً، لأن تلك القدرة توجد مع الفعل فكأنك قلت: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو فاعل لها وهذا محال. ثم يقابل بمثل هذا التشنيع فيقال: هل أعطى الله تعالى وليا قوة الطاعة بعين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، تمكنت وحشة عظيمة، وإن قال: نعم، أقر بقولنا، فإن قالوا: لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول: لو قيل له لم لا فعلت كذا؟ فيقول: لأنني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب. قلنا: هذا يكون عذرًا فيما منع عنه القدرة لا فيما ضيعها بإيثاره بدله.
As for their statement: 'Did anyone avoid the disobedience of God while being capable of it (qādir ʿalayhā)?' We say: If you mean by power (al-qudra) the power of instruments (ālāt) and causes (asbāb), then yes. If you mean the true power of the act (ḥaqīqat qudrat al-fiʿl), then the question is impossible, because that power exists with the act. It is as if you said: 'Did anyone avoid the disobedience of God while being a doer of it?' And this is impossible. Then it is countered with a similar objection, and it is said: Did God give a saint the power of obedience (quwwat al-ṭāʿa) identical to the obedience itself? If he says no, a great unease (waḥsha ʿaẓīma) arises. If he says yes, he affirms our statement. Then they say: There is no excuse for the servant in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) greater than his saying, when it is said to him 'Why did you not do such-and-such?' 'Because I was not able to do it.' So the like of it in the unseen (al-ghāʾib). We say: This is an excuse regarding what one is prevented from by power, not regarding what one wasted by preferring something else instead.
Source pages p. 847

Commentary
This passage handles an objection and a counter-objection. The objectors ask: Can someone avoid sin while having power over it? Nasafi answers: If by 'power' you mean the capacity of instruments and causes, then yes; if you mean the real power of the act itself, the question is impossible because that power exists only with the act—it would mean avoiding sin while doing it, a contradiction. Then he counters with a similar objection: Did God give a saint the power of obedience identical to the obedience? If they say no, a great unease arises; if yes, they agree with us. Then they argue that no excuse is greater in the witnessed world than saying 'I was not able,' and analogously in the unseen. Our response: That is an excuse only for what one is prevented from, not for what one wastes by preferring something else.

وحشة
عذر
شاهد
غائب

Misconception: Some think that claiming inability is always a valid excuse.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that inability excuses only when one is genuinely prevented, not when one wastes the ability by choosing something else.

Checkpoint

A) When one had the power but chose otherwise
B) When one is prevented from the act
C) Always
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Nasafi teaches that the excuse of inability is only valid when one is truly prevented, not when one squanders the opportunity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Faith and Disbelief

Refuting the Claim that Disbelief is God's Action
Nasafi refutes the objection that disbelief is God's action by redefining the kafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the claim that disbelief is directly God's action by distinguishing between action (fi'l) and effect (maf'ūl), and by redefining the disbeliever as one in whom disbelief resides.

Core Idea
The disbeliever is not defined by performing disbelief, but by having disbelief in them.

This passage tackles a common objection: if disbelief is a human action, and all actions are created by God, then God is the creator of disbelief. Nasafi responds by making a key distinction between an action as the agent's own doing (fi'l) and as God's created effect (maf'ūl). He then strengthens his position by citing the Najjariyya and Ahl al-Hadith who define the disbeliever purely by the attribute residing in them, avoiding the dilemma entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concession at the beginning: 'We concede this.'
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where Nasafi distinguishes between fi'l (action) and maf'ūl (effect). Understand why disbelief is the disbeliever's fi'l but God's maf'ūl.
This distinction is central to the refutation.
3
Understand the alternative definition
Look at the definition given by the Najjariyya and Ahl al-Hadith: the kafir is 'man qāma bihi al-kufr' (one in whom disbelief resides), not 'man fa'ala al-kufr' (one who performs disbelief). Reflect on the analogies with movement, death, etc.
4
Grasp the dilemma
Nasafi forces the opponent into a choice: either attribute every created quality (tall, short, etc.) to God as His action, leading to blasphemy, or admit their position is contradictory.
This is a powerful rhetorical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجابون عن قولهم: إن الكافر من فعله الكفر. قلنا: نحن نسلم هذا، ولكن عندنا الكفر فعل الكافر لا فعل الله تعالى وإنما هو مفعوله، ومن سلم لكم أن الكفر فعل الله تعالى من النجارية ومتكلمي أهل الحديث فإنهم يمنعون أن يكون الكافر من كان الكفر فعله ويقولون : الكافر من كان الكفر فعله، ويقولون: من قام به الكفر لا من فعل الكفر كما أن المتحرك من قامت به الحركة لا من فعل الحركة والميت من قام به الموت لا من فعل الموت، وكذا هذا في السواد والبياض والحرارة والبرودة والحلاوة والحموضة والطول والقصر، فإما ما تزعمون أن الميت المريض الطويل القصير الأسود الأبيض الحار البارد الحلو الحامض هو الله تعالى لكون هذه المعانى فعلاً له فتنسلخون عن الدين، وإما أن تقروا بتناقض مذهبكم وبطلان شبهتكم.
And by this they are answered concerning their statement: 'The disbeliever is one whose action is disbelief.' We say: We concede this, but according to us, disbelief is the action of the disbeliever, not the action of God, rather it is His effect. And those who concede to you that disbelief is the action of God—from the Najjariyya and the theologians of Ahl al-Hadith—they indeed prevent that the disbeliever is one whose action is disbelief, and they say: The disbeliever is one in whom disbelief resides, not one who performs disbelief, just as the moving thing is that in which movement resides, not that which performs movement; and the dead is that in which death resides, not that which performs death. And likewise for blackness, whiteness, heat, cold, sweetness, sourness, length, and shortness. Then either you claim that the dead, the sick, the tall, the short, the black, the white, the hot, the cold, the sweet, the sour is God, because these meanings are His action—so you divest yourselves of religion—or you admit to the contradiction of your doctrine and the invalidity of your argument.
Source pages p. 923

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that since disbelief is the disbeliever's action, it must be created by God, thus making God responsible for disbelief. Nasafi first concedes that disbelief is the action of the disbeliever, but he distinguishes between action (fi'l) as what the agent does and effect (maf'ūl) as what is created. According to him, disbelief is the disbeliever's act (fi'l) but God's effect (maf'ūl). He then cites the Najjariyya and Ahl al-Hadith who deny that disbelief is God's action at all; instead, they define the disbeliever as one in whom disbelief occurs (qāma bihi al-kufr), not one who performs disbelief. This is analogous to movement: the moving thing is that in which movement resides, not that which performs movement. Nasafi then forces the opponent to a dilemma: either they attribute all created qualities (like tall, short, etc.) to God as His actions, leading to blasphemy, or they admit their position is contradictory.

Key Terms
الكافر
الكفر
فعل
مفعول
النجارية
أهل الحديث
قام به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if disbelief is an action, it must be directly created by God, making God responsible for disbelief.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that while disbelief is the human's action (fi'l), it is only God's effect (maf'ūl), meaning God creates the act as a phenomenon but the moral responsibility lies with the human. Alternatively, one can define the disbeliever as the one in whom disbelief resides, avoiding the attribution of the act to God entirely.

Checkpoint
How do the Najjariyya and Ahl al-Hadith define the disbeliever?
A) The one who performs disbelief
B) The one in whom disbelief resides
C) The one who creates disbelief
D) The one who denies God's actions

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the objection by clarifying that while disbelief is the human's action, it is God's creation only as an effect, and the true definition of a disbeliever is one in whom disbelief resides, not one who performs it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your own sins by saying 'God created my actions'? How does Nasafi's distinction between your action (fi'l) and God's creation (maf'ūl) confront that excuse and restore your personal accountability before Allah?
?
The passage defines the disbeliever by what resides in him, not by his deeds. What qualities reside in your heart right now—faith, hypocrisy, or something else? Will you honestly examine yourself today and ask Allah to purify what resides in you?
?
Nasafi's dilemma forces the opponent to either blaspheme or admit contradiction. Where in your own understanding of God's role in your life do you hold a contradiction? Will you resolve it by submitting to the truth of this lesson before you face Allah with that inconsistency?

Introduction to Names and Rulings
Introduces the new topic of how to name and judge major sinners among Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central question of the Islamic theological debate on the status of major sinners among Muslims.

Core Idea
The debate on 'Names and Rulings' asks: what do we call a Muslim who commits a major sin, and what is their fate?

This passage opens a new section of the text, marking a shift from broader theological foundations to a specific issue that divided early Islamic sects. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, frames the debate by stating that people disagreed about both the name and the final judgment of those who commit major sins. As you read, note that the passage only introduces the problem; it does not yet provide the Maturidi answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the Arabic and translation to grasp the topic. Note the key terms: 'al-asma' wa al-ahkam' (names and rulings) and 'al-wa'd wa al-wa'id' (promise and threat).
The Arabic is concise; the translation helps clarify.
2
Identify the two questions
The passage asks two things: by what name are major sinners called, and what is their judgment in the afterlife. This is the core of the debate.
3
Understand the context
Recognize that this topic is historically contentious between Kharijites, Mu'tazila, and Ahl al-Sunna. The passage sets up the Maturidi position which will be elaborated later.
This is just the introduction; answers come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الأسماء والأحكام والوعد والوعيد. اختلف الناس في مقترفي الكبائر من أهل القبلة أنهم بماذا يسمون وأن حكمهم في الآخرة ما هو؟
Discussion on Names and Rulings (al-asma' wa al-ahkam) and Promise and Threat (al-wa'd wa al-wa'id). People differed regarding those who commit major sins (kaba'ir) among the people of the Qibla: by what name are they called, and what is their judgment in the afterlife?
Source pages p. 1042

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new major section in the book. The author announces the topic: 'al-asma' wa al-ahkam wa al-wa'd wa al-wa'id' (Names and Rulings, and Promise and Threat). This is a classic theological discussion about the status of Muslims who commit major sins (al-kaba'ir). The central question is twofold: (1) What term should be used for such individuals? Are they still believers (mu'minun), or do they become sinners (fussaq), or something else? (2) What will be their final judgment in the Hereafter? Will they be punished eternally or temporarily? The author notes that people (i.e., different Islamic sects) have disagreed on this. This sets up the subsequent elaboration of the Maturidi position, which will be distinguished from the Kharijites (who deem grave sinners as unbelievers), the Mu'tazila (who place them in a middle position), and others.

الوعد والوعيد (al-wa'd wa al-wa'id)
الكبائر (al-kaba'ir)
أهل القبلة (ahl al-qiblah)

Orthodox View on Major Sinners and Their Fate
Al-Nasafi presents the orthodox Sunni view on major sinners and their fate in the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the orthodox Sunni position on the naming and ultimate destiny of believers who commit major sins, including the role of God's will, intercession, and the meaning of irja'.

Core Idea
Major sinners remain believers; their final fate is in God's hands, but they ultimately enter Paradise.

This lesson examines the classical Sunni response to a critical theological question: what happens to a Muslim who commits a major sin? Al-Nasafi presents the balanced view of Ahl al-Haqq, affirming both the sinner's retained faith and the possibility of punishment, while ultimately securing Paradise. The passage also clarifies the misunderstood label of 'Murji'' applied to Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Understand the theological question
The passage opens by stating the disagreement: what to call those who commit major sins among the people of the Qibla, and what is their judgment in the Hereafter? Keep this question in mind.
2
Learn the definition of the sinner
Ahl al-Haqq defines the person as one who commits a major sin without considering it lawful or belittling it, due to desire or zeal, hoping for forgiveness and fearing punishment. This person is still called a believer.
The key is that faith is not removed by the sin.
3
Grasp the judgment in the Hereafter
If such a person dies without repentance, God has discretion (mashi'a): He may forgive by His bounty or through intercession, or He may punish according to the sin. But the final outcome is Paradise, not eternal Hell.
This balances mercy and justice.
4
Clarify the 'Murji'' label
The passage explains why Abu Hanifa was called a Murji': because he deferred judgment to God's will. It then shows that this deferment is rooted in the angels' humble statement in the Quran.
Irja' means deferment, not a denial of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الأسماء والأحكام والوعد والوعيد. اختلف الناس في مقترفي الكبائر من أهل القبلة أنهم بماذا يسمون وأن حكمهم في الآخرة ما هو؟ قال أهل الحق: إن من اقترف كبيرة غير مستحل لها ولا مستخف بها لغلبة شهوة أو حمية يرجو الله تعالى أن يغفر له ويخاف أن يعذبه فهذا اسمه مؤمن، بقي على ما كان عليه من الإيمان لم يزل عنه إيمانه ولم ينتقض، ولا يخرج أحد من الإيمان إلا من الباب الذي دخل فيه، فحكمه أنه لو مات من غير توبة فلله فيه المشيئة إن شاء عفا عنه بفضله وكرمه أو يتركه وما معه من الإيمان والحسنات أو بشفاعة بعض الأخيار، وإن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة ولا يخلد في النار، وكان أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يسمى مرجئًا لتأخيره أمر صاحب الكبيرة إلى مشيئة الله تعالى، والإرجاء هو التأخير، وروى عنه أنه قيل له ممن أخذت الإرجاء، فقال: من الملائكة عليهم السلام حيث قالوا: لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا
Discussion on names, judgments, promise, and threat. People differed concerning those among the people of the Qibla who commit major sins: by what name are they called, and what is their judgment in the Hereafter? The People of Truth said: Whoever commits a major sin without considering it lawful and without belittling it, due to overwhelming desire or zeal, hoping that God will forgive him and fearing that He will punish him, then this one is called a believer. He remains upon what he was of faith; his faith has not left him nor has it been invalidated. And no one leaves faith except through the door by which he entered. His judgment is that if he dies without repentance, God has discretion concerning him: if He wills, He pardons him by His bounty and generosity, or He leaves him with what he has of faith and good deeds, or by the intercession of some of the righteous; and if He wills, He punishes him according to his sin, then his final outcome is Paradise without doubt, and he will not abide eternally in the Fire. And Abu Hanifa - may God have mercy on him - was called a Murji' because he deferred the matter of the grave sinner to God's will, and 'irja'' means deferment. And it is reported from him that he was asked: From whom did you take irja'? He said: From the angels, peace be upon them, when they said: 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.'
Source pages p. 1042

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the orthodox Sunni position on the naming and ultimate fate of Muslims who commit major sins. It begins with the heading 'Discussion on Names, Judgments, Promise, and Threat' and states the point of contention: what to call such people and what their final judgment is. Then it gives the view of Ahl al-Haqq (People of Truth). It defines the person as one who commits a major sin without istihlal (deeming it lawful) or istikhfaf (belittling it), but due to overwhelming desire or zeal, and who fears God's punishment and hopes for His mercy. This person remains a believer; his faith is not removed. He only exits faith through the same door he entered, meaning by renouncing belief itself. The judgment for such a person who dies unrepentant is that God has the will (mashi'a) to either forgive him out of His bounty or to punish him according to his sin, but his final destination is Paradise, and he will not abide eternally in Hell. The passage also addresses the labeling of Abu Hanifa as a Murji' (one who postpones judgment) because he deferred the fate of the grave sinner to God's will, explaining that 'irja' means deferment. Abu Hanifa traced this deferment to the angels' statement in the Quran (2:32), showing that it is a humble acknowledgment of limited knowledge.

الكبيرة
الإيمان
المشيئة
الشفاعة
الخلود
الإرجاء
أبو حنيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the orthodox view is identical to the Murji'a position of no punishment at all.
Correction: The passage clearly states that punishment is possible under God's will, though the final outcome is Paradise.

Checkpoint

C) He is considered a hypocrite and will be in the lowest depths of Hell
D) He is no longer a believer and will be in Hell forever

Key Takeaway
A believer's major sin does not negate faith; God has discretion, but the ultimate outcome is Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Faith and Disbelief

The Evil Murji'a View on Major Sins
Al-Nasafi presents and refutes the extreme Murji'ite claim that no Muslim is punished for any major sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme Murji'ite position that faith renders major sins harmless, and why it is condemned as contrary to orthodoxy.

Core Idea
Faith does not make sin harmless; the evil Murji'a err by claiming it does.

Al-Nasafi wastes no time in condemning a radical sectarian view: that a Muslim who commits major sins will never be punished because faith itself neutralizes evil deeds. This was the claim of the extreme Murji'a, attributed to the commentator Muqatil ibn Sulayman. Understanding this position helps clarify the orthodox Ahl al-Sunna stance on sin and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the exact wording of the Murji'ite claim.
Notice the strong language: 'evil Murji'a' indicates this is a rejected view.
2
Analyze the analogy
Identify the two-part analogy: good deeds useless with disbelief, evil deeds harmless with faith. Understand why this analogy is flawed from an orthodox perspective.
3
Consider the source attribution
Reflect on why Al-Nasafi names Muqatil ibn Sulayman. This is often done to discredit the opinion by linking it to a specific figure.
Muqatil's tafsir was influential but his views were criticized.
4
Contrast with orthodox belief
Recall or infer the Ahl al-Sunna position: major sins are dangerous and may lead to punishment unless repented, though faith prevents eternal damnation.
This will be covered in more detail in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت المرجئة الخبيثة أن أحدًا من المسلمين لا يعاقب على شيء من الكبائر، وكما أن الحسنة لا تنفع مع الكفر فالسيئة لا تضر مع الإيمان حكى هذا القول عن مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير.
And the evil Murji'a claimed that none of the Muslims will be punished for any of the major sins, and just as a good deed does not benefit with disbelief, so an evil deed does not harm with faith. This opinion is reported from Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the author of the Tafsir.
Source pages p. 1043

Commentary
This brief passage presents the extreme Murji'ite position, which is contrary to the orthodox view. The author labels them as 'evil' (khabitha). Their claim is twofold: first, that no Muslim will suffer punishment for any major sin; second, they draw an analogy: just as good deeds are useless alongside disbelief, so evil deeds are harmless alongside faith. In other words, faith renders sin ineffective. The author attributes this view to Muqatil ibn Sulayman, known for his tafsir, implying that this is an erroneous opinion.

Key Terms
المرجئة
الكبائر
الإيمان
الحسنة
السيئة
مقاتل بن سليمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that all Murji'ites deny any punishment for sins, but only the extreme faction (like Muqatil) held this radical view; mainstream Murji'a deferred judgment without negating punishment.
Correction: The text specifically targets the 'evil Murji'a' who claim no punishment at all, not all Murji'a.

Checkpoint
According to the extreme Murji'a as presented in the text, what protects a Muslim from punishment for major sins?
A) Repentance
B) Faith
C) Good deeds
D) Divine mercy alone

Key Takeaway
The extreme Murji'ite claim that faith makes major sins harmless is condemned by Al-Nasafi as a false and evil doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'I'm a believer, so this small sin won't really matter'? How does the Murji'a claim in this lesson mirror that attitude, and what would Al-Nasafi say to you?
?
In your daily life, which area of sin do you most rely on your faith to excuse? Will you still make that excuse after reading this passage?
?
If you truly believed that every major sin you commit has consequences even with faith, how would you change what you do before sleeping tonight?

Mu'tazilite Doctrine of the Intermediate State
Al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite position on the intermediate state and eternal punishment for major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that a major sinner is neither a believer nor a disbeliever but occupies an intermediate state, and that such a person will be eternally punished in Hell if unrepentant.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny the label of believer to grave sinners, placing them in a middle category with eternal punishment.

The Mu'tazilite school of theology held distinctive views on the status of those who commit major sins. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi summarizes their position: such a person is not a believer, not a disbeliever, but a fasiq in an intermediate station. Their fate, if they die without repentance, is eternal punishment in Hell. This view contrasts sharply with orthodox Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the three claims
The passage makes three distinct claims: (1) the label of the grave sinner is 'fasiq', not believer or disbeliever; (2) such a person has an intermediate station between faith and disbelief; (3) if he dies unrepentant, he will be eternally in Hell and his faith will not help.
Notice the logical sequence: naming, status, consequence.
3
Contrast with the orthodox view
Reflect on how this differs from the standard Sunni position that a grave sinner remains a believer and is subject to God's will—possible forgiveness or temporary punishment.
This comparison will be elaborated in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : إن اسم هذا فاسق ولا يسمى مؤمنا ولا كافرًا، وله منزل بين منزلتي الإيمان والكفر، وحكمه أنه لو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه
And the Mu'tazila said: The name of this one is fasiq; he is neither called a believer nor a disbeliever. And he has an intermediate station between the two stations of faith and disbelief. And his judgment is that if he dies before repentance, he will abide eternally in the Fire, and his faith will not benefit him.
Source pages p. 1043

Commentary
This brief passage outlines the Mu'tazilite position on the naming and fate of the grave sinner. They assign him the label fasiq, rejecting both the labels of believer and disbeliever. This is their famous doctrine of 'the intermediate position' (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn). Unlike the orthodox view that he remains a believer and is subject to God's will, the Mu'tazila assert that if he dies unrepentant, he will be eternally in Hell, and his faith is of no avail. This is a direct contrast to the orthodox view of possible forgiveness and eventual Paradise.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الفسق
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
الخلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite 'intermediate position' is similar to the orthodox view that a grave sinner is still a believer with sin.
Correction: In fact, the Mu'tazila deny the label of believer entirely to the grave sinner and assign him an intermediate status, which is distinct from the orthodox position.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the fate of a person who commits a major sin and dies without repentance?
A) He will be forgiven by God's mercy
B) He will be punished temporarily and then enter Paradise
C) He will abide eternally in Hell
D) He will be in a state of limbo

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view the grave sinner as a fasiq in an intermediate state who, if unrepentant, will suffer eternal punishment in Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly rely on your faith alone to save you, regardless of unrepented sins? The Mu'tazila said faith cannot benefit an unrepentant sinner—are you certain that your trust in God's mercy is not presumption?
?
If God were to judge you this moment, would you be content to be called a 'fasiq' rather than a 'believer'? This lesson strips away comfortable labels—what label truly fits your inner state?
?
The Mu'tazila made the grave sinner an in-between category. But in your heart, do you believe your own sins place you closer to the disbelievers than to the prophets? How does that belief affect your daily choices?

The Grave Sinner: Believer and His Ultimate Fate
Ahl al-Haqq define the grave sinner and his final fate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni definition of a grave sinner as a believer and the conditional nature of his punishment.

Core Idea
A grave sinner remains a believer; his final home is Paradise.

This lesson presents the core teaching of Ahl al-Haqq regarding those who commit major sins. It clarifies that such a person is not stripped of faith, and his ultimate fate rests with Allah's mercy and justice. The passage establishes a balanced position between despair and presumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the precise conditions given for the grave sinner.
2
Identify the definition and conditions
Find the description of the grave sinner: what he does not do (deem lawful, treat lightly), why he sins (overwhelming desire/zeal), and his attitude (hope and fear).
These conditions are crucial for the ruling.
3
Understand the ruling on his fate
Examine the statement about his status as a believer and the options after death: forgiveness, intercession, or punishment, but ultimate Paradise.
This contrasts with both extremists who guarantee hell and those who guarantee paradise without repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي أهل الحق
قال أهل الحق: إن من اقترف كبيرة غير مستحل لها ولا مستخف بها لغلبة شهوة أو حمية يرجو الله تعالى أن يغفر له ويخاف أن يعذبه فهذا اسمه مؤمن، بقي على ما كان عليه من الإيمان لم يزل عنه إيمانه ولم ينتقض، ولا يخرج أحد الإيمان إلا من الباب الذي دخل فيه، فحكمه أنه لو مات من غير توبة فلله فيه المشيئة إن شاء عفا عنه بفضله وكرمه أو يتركه وما معه من الإيمان والحسنات أو بشفاعة بعض الأخيار، وإن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة ولا يخلد في النار، وكان أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يسمى مرجئًا لتأخيره أمر صاحب الكبيرة إلى مشيئة الله تعالى، والإرجاء هو التأخير، وروى عنه أنه قيل له ممن أخذت الإرجاء، فقال: من الملائكة عليهم السلام حيث قالوا: لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا وزعمت المرجئة الخبيثة أن أحدًا من المسلمين لا يعاقب على شيء من الكبائر، وكما أن الحسنة لا تنفع مع الكفر فالسيئة لا تضر مع الإيمان حكى هذا القول عن مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير.
The view of Ahl al-Haqq: Ahl al-Haqq said: Whoever commits a major sin without deeming it lawful nor treating it lightly due to overwhelming desire or zeal, hoping that Allah will forgive him and fearing that He will punish him, then his name is a believer. He remains upon what he was upon of faith; his faith has not left him nor is it invalidated. No one leaves faith except through the door by which he entered. Its ruling: if he dies without repentance, then concerning him is the will of Allah. If He wills, He forgives him by His bounty and generosity, or He leaves him with what he has of faith and good deeds, or through the intercession of some righteous ones. If He wills, He punishes him according to his sin; then the final outcome of his affair is Paradise undoubtedly, and he will not abide eternally in the Fire. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, was called Murji' due to deferring the matter of the grave sinner to the will of Allah. Al-Irja' is deferment. It is narrated that he was asked: From whom did you take irja'? He said: From the angels, peace be upon them, when they said: 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.' The evil Murji'a claimed that none of the Muslims will be punished for any major sin, and just as a good deed does not benefit with disbelief, so a sin does not harm with faith. This statement is narrated from Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the commentator.
Source pages p. 1043

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth, i.e., Sunni Muslims) regarding the grave sinner (sāḥib al-kabīra). It defines the grave sinner as one who commits a major sin without deeming it lawful or being disdainful, but rather due to overpowering desire or zeal, while still hoping for forgiveness and fearing punishment. Such a person remains a believer; his faith is not nullified. The ruling for one who dies without repentance is that his ultimate fate is left to God's will: He may forgive by His grace, accept intercession, or punish proportionally, but the final destination is Paradise, not eternal hellfire. The passage then addresses the label 'Murji'a' applied to Abu Hanifa due to his deferring judgment to God's will. Abu Hanifa refutes the extremist Murji'a who claim that sin does not harm faith, attributing that view to Muqatil ibn Sulayman. The passage thus clarifies the orthodox position against both the extremists and their accusers.

Key Terms
كبيرة
مؤمن
إيمان
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Haqq guarantee Paradise for all grave sinners regardless of repentance.
Correction: The passage states that the final outcome is Paradise, but it also mentions the possibility of punishment according to the sin. Repentance is not mentioned here, but punishment is a real possibility.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what is the ultimate outcome for a grave sinner who dies without repentance?
A) Eternal hellfire
B) Paradise undoubtedly
C) He remains in a state between faith and disbelief
D) He is a hypocrite in the lowest depths of hell

Key Takeaway
A Muslim who commits a major sin without deeming it lawful remains a believer, and his final destination is Paradise, though he may be punished first.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly fear Allah’s punishment and hope for His forgiveness as this passage describes, or have you become desensitized to sin? What will you do today to revive that fear and hope?
?
This lesson says that even a grave sinner may be punished before entering Paradise. Are you living as though you are immune to that punishment, assuming your faith alone guarantees immediate paradise? What would change if you took that possibility seriously?
?
The passage mentions intercession as a means of forgiveness for grave sinners. Do you rely more on the intercession of others than on your own repentance and turning back to Allah? How can you balance hope in intercession with personal accountability?

The Murji'a Label and Extremist Views
Abu Hanifa and the Murji'a dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the origin of the label 'Murji'a' applied to Abu Hanifa and the refutation of extremist Murji'a views.

Core Idea
Irja' means deferring judgment to Allah, not denying sin's harm.

This lesson addresses the historical context of the term 'Murji'a'. It shows how Abu Hanifa was called Murji'a because he left the fate of the grave sinner to God's will, and how he derived this concept from the angels. It also refutes the extremist claim that sin does not harm faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Note the shift from the general ruling to the historical controversy.
2
Understand the meaning of irja'
Find the definition of irja' given in the passage and how Abu Hanifa explained its origin.
Irja' comes from the angels' statement in the Quran.
3
Identify the extremist view and its refutation
Look for the claim of the evil Murji'a and the attribution to Muqatil ibn Sulayman. Understand why it is rejected.
They claimed sin does not harm faith, which contradicts the earlier ruling.

The Grave Sinner's Station: Mu'tazila Doctrine
Mu'tazila define the grave sinner as a fasiq in an intermediate state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila concept of the intermediate station (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) for the grave sinner and its consequences.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila place the grave sinner between faith and disbelief, but with a severe eternal fate.

The Mu'tazila, an early theological school, introduced a unique category for those who commit major sins but still profess faith. Unlike the Kharijites who deem them disbelievers and the Murji'ah who consider them believers, the Mu'tazila assign them an intermediate status. This passage outlines their position and its implications for the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the three key claims: the naming (fasiq), the station (manzila), and the ruling (hukm).
Notice that the passage explicitly says 'not called a believer nor a disbeliever'.
2
Identify the terminology
Identify the three terms: 'fasiq', 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn', and 'khuld fi al-nar'. Understand each term's significance.
3
Reflect on the logical consequence
Consider why the Mu'tazila argue that forgiveness after death without repentance is not from wisdom. This reflects their view on divine justice.
This step connects the passage to broader Mu'tazila theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : إن اسم هذا فاسق ولا يسمى مؤمنا ولا كافرًا، وله منزل بين منزلتي الإيمان والكفر، وحكمه أنه لو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه وطاعته وليس من الحكمة عفوه ومغفرته، وأما صاحب الصغيرة فهو عندهم مؤمن ولو اجتنب الكبائر لاستحق مغفرة الصغائر ولا يجوز الله تعذيبه عليها.
The Mu'tazila said: The name of this person is a disobedient sinner (fāsiq); he is not called a believer nor a disbeliever. He has a station between the station of faith and disbelief. Its ruling: if he dies before repentance, he will abide eternally in the Fire, and his faith and obedience will not benefit him, and it is not from wisdom to pardon him and forgive him. As for the committer of a minor sin, he is in their view a believer, and if he avoids major sins he deserves forgiveness for minor sins, and it is not permissible that Allah punish him for them.
Source pages p. 1043

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine on the grave sinner. They label him a fāsiq (disobedient sinner) and assign him a status between belief and unbelief (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). His ruling if he dies without repentance is eternal hellfire, and his faith does not save him. They also hold that God cannot forgive him because it would contradict wisdom. For minor sins, the person remains a believer and if he avoids major sins, he deserves forgiveness and God cannot punish him for them.

Key Terms
فاسق
منزلة بين منزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila consider the grave sinner a disbeliever.
Correction: The passage explicitly states he is not called a disbeliever (kafir), but rather a fasiq in an intermediate state.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the grave sinner in Mu'tazila theology?
A) He is a disbeliever (kafir)
B) He is a believer (mu'min)
C) He is a fasiq in an intermediate station
D) He is a hypocrite (munafiq)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila categorize the grave sinner as a fasiq in an intermediate state, with eternal punishment if unrepentant, reflecting their strict view of divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you died tonight with a major sin you never repented from, would you feel you deserve eternal punishment? How does the Mu'tazila insistence on justice without 'easy forgiveness' challenge your assumptions about God's mercy? Do you need to examine your view of sin?
?
The Mu'tazila claim it is 'not from wisdom' for God to pardon an unrepentant grave sinner. Do you think God's wisdom could ever allow forgiveness without repentance? What does your answer reveal about how you balance hope in mercy and fear of justice?
?
Imagine you are the grave sinner described: you have faith and good deeds, but you die with a major sin unrepented. According to this teaching, your faith and obedience 'will not benefit you.' If that were true, would you still consider yourself safe? What does that tell you about the urgency of repentance right now?

Minor Sins According to the Mu'tazila
Mu'tazila hold that the committer of a minor sin remains a believer and deserves forgiveness if avoiding major sins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila view on minor sins and how they differ from major sins in their theology.

Core Idea
Minor sins do not remove one from faith if major sins are avoided; forgiveness is deserved.

In contrast to their strict stance on major sins, the Mu'tazila maintain that minor sins are forgivable and do not affect one's status as a believer, provided one avoids major sins. This passage completes the picture of their theology of sin and faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key condition: 'if he avoids major sins'.
2
Analyze the condition and consequence
Identify what the Mu'tazila say about the minor sinner's faith and the deserved forgiveness. Compare with the previous lesson on major sins.
Notice the phrase 'لا يجوز' – it is not permissible for Allah to punish, showing an obligation on God's part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : إن اسم هذا فاسق ولا يسمى مؤمنا ولا كافرًا، وله منزل بين منزلتي الإيمان والكفر، وحكمه أنه لو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه وطاعته وليس من الحكمة عفوه ومغفرته، وأما صاحب الصغيرة فهو عندهم مؤمن ولو اجتنب الكبائر لاستحق مغفرة الصغائر ولا يجوز الله تعذيبه عليها.
The Mu'tazila said: The name of this person is a disobedient sinner (fāsiq); he is not called a believer nor a disbeliever. He has a station between the station of faith and disbelief. Its ruling: if he dies before repentance, he will abide eternally in the Fire, and his faith and obedience will not benefit him, and it is not from wisdom to pardon him and forgive him. As for the committer of a minor sin, he is in their view a believer, and if he avoids major sins he deserves forgiveness for minor sins, and it is not permissible that Allah punish him for them.
Source pages p. 1043

This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine on the grave sinner. They label him a fāsiq (disobedient sinner) and assign him a status between belief and unbelief (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). His ruling if he dies without repentance is eternal hellfire, and his faith does not save him. They also hold that God cannot forgive him because it would contradict wisdom. For minor sins, the person remains a believer and if he avoids major sins, he deserves forgiveness and God cannot punish him for them.

كبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He is a fasiq

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat minor sins as trivial because you think they are automatically forgiven? How does the Mu'tazila condition that forgiveness depends on avoiding major sins challenge your attitude today?
?
On a scale of 1 to 10, how conscious are you of avoiding major sins in your daily life? What is one major sin you have been neglecting to avoid, and will you commit to avoiding it starting now?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tonight, would you be confident that you have avoided major sins such that your minor sins are forgiven? What concrete change will you make in the next 24 hours to move toward that confidence?

Khawarij Views on the Grave Sinner
Khawarij and their sub-sects define the grave sinner as a disbeliever or other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of Khawarij views on the grave sinner and how some early figures diverged from the majority.

Core Idea
The Khawarij were not monolithic; they held varying views on the fate of the grave sinner.

The Khawarij are often caricatured as holding that any sin equals disbelief. However, this passage from al-Nasafi shows internal diversity: some equate grave sin with polytheism, others distinguish minor from major, and still others allow temporary punishment. We also see the positions of Najda al-Hanafi, Ghilan al-Qadari, and al-Hasan al-Basri, showing that the debate extended beyond the Khawarij.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Get an overall sense of the different opinions presented.
The passage lists multiple views in a compact form.
2
Identify the majority view and its variations
The majority says any sin is disbelief leading to eternal hell. Note the two variations: some say polytheism, others differentiate minor and major sins.
3
Examine Najda al-Hanafi's position
Najda al-Hanafi differs by saying the grave sinner is punished temporarily and then enters Paradise without pardon. This is a distinct view within the Khawarij.
Notice that he rejects eternal hellfire for the grave sinner.
4
Consider the views of Ghilan and al-Hasan
Ghilan al-Qadari allows pardon but with a logical restriction. Al-Hasan al-Basri considers the grave sinner a hypocrite. Reflect on how these views broaden the discussion beyond the Khawarij.
These figures are not Khawarij but weigh in on the same issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول جمهور الخوارج: إن من عصى الله تعالى فقد كفر فكان كافرًا وحكمه أنه يخلد في النار صغيرة كان من فعل أم كبيرة، ومنهم من يقول هو مشرك ومنهم من فرق بين الصغيرة وبين الكبيرة ويحكم بكفره وتخليده في النار بارتكابه الكبيرة دون الصغيرة، وحكى عن نجدة الحنفي صاحب النجدات من الخوارج أن صاحب الكبيرة لا يخلد بل يعذب لا محالة بقدر ذنبه ولا يجوز العفو عنه ثم كان عاقبة أمره دخول الجنة، وحكى عن غيلان القدري أنه كان يقول: إن العفو عن صاحب الكبيرة جائراً إلا أنه تعالى إذا عفا عن أحد ارتكب كبيرة لم يجز له تعذيب غيره على تلك الكبيرة، وحكى عن الحسن البصري كان يقول: إن صاحب الكبيرة منافق.
The saying of the majority of the Khawarij: Whoever disobeys Allah has disbelieved, so he is a disbeliever, and its ruling is that he will abide eternally in the Fire, whether the sin is minor or major. And among them are those who say he is a polytheist. And among them are those who differentiate between the minor and major sin, and rule that he is a disbeliever and will abide eternally in the Fire for committing the major sin, not the minor. And it is narrated from Najda al-Hanafi, leader of the Najdat among the Khawarij, that the grave sinner does not abide eternally but is certainly punished according to his sin, and pardon is not permissible, then the outcome of his affair is entering Paradise. And it is narrated from Ghilan al-Qadari that he used to say: Pardon for the grave sinner is permissible, except that if Allah pardons one who committed a major sin, He is not permitted to punish another for that same sin. And it is narrated from al-Hasan al-Basri that he used to say: The grave sinner is a hypocrite.
Source pages p. 1044

Commentary
This passage presents the diverse views of the Khawarij and some early figures on the grave sinner. The majority of Khawarij hold that any sin, minor or major, constitutes disbelief and results in eternal hellfire. Some say the sinner becomes a polytheist (mushrik). Others distinguish between minor and major sins, ruling that only major sins lead to disbelief and eternity in hell. Najda al-Hanafi, a Kharijite leader, holds that the grave sinner is punished temporarily but ultimately enters Paradise without pardon. Ghilan al-Qadari allows pardon but limits it so that if God pardons one person, He cannot punish another for the same sin. Al-Hasan al-Basri is reported to have considered the grave sinner a hypocrite (munāfiq).

Misconception: All Khawarij believe that any sin leads to eternal hellfire.
Correction: The passage shows diversity: some Khawarij hold that only major sins lead to eternal hell, and Najda al-Hanafi believes the grave sinner eventually enters Paradise.

The Khawarij Proof from Quran 4:14
Khawarij cite Quran 4:14 as proof for excommunication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Khawarij used Quran 4:14 as scriptural evidence for their doctrine of excommunicating all sinners.

Core Idea
One verse, one extreme conclusion: the Khawarij's takfir of all sinners.

The Khawarij are known for their extreme position that any sin, minor or major, constitutes disbelief. To support this, they turned to a specific verse from the Quran. This lesson examines how they interpreted Quran 4:14 as proof for their doctrine, and why this interpretation is contested.

Guided Path
1

Understand that the Khawarij held that any disobedience to Allah results in kufr (disbelief).
This is the extreme position that this verse is used to support.
2

3
Analyze the implication
Consider why the Khawarij saw eternal punishment as evidence that the sinner is a disbeliever.
Think about the logical link between eternal punishment and disbelief in their reasoning.
4
Recognize the limitation
Reflect on how other scholars might counter this argument using other verses about forgiveness.
This verse does not explicitly say the sinner is a disbeliever; it only mentions eternal punishment.
